Trapped particles, by the mirror effect due to $B(\\theta)$ variation, execute bounce motion along field lines, with a bounce frequency for deeply trapped particles scaling as\n$$\n\\omega_b \\sim \\frac{v}{q R} \\sqrt{\\epsilon}.\n$$\nThe trapped fraction in a large-aspect-ratio tokamak scales as\n$$\nf_t \\sim \\sqrt{\\epsilon}.\n$$\n\nWe now estimate the characteristic radial excursion (banana width) of a trapped particle per bounce by the ratio of the drift speed to the bounce frequency:\n$$\n\\Delta_b \\sim \\frac{v_d}{\\omega_b} \\sim \\frac{v^2/(\\Omega R)}{v/(q R)\\sqrt{\\epsilon}} = \\frac{v}{\\Omega} \\frac{q}{\\sqrt{\\epsilon}} = \\frac{q}{\\sqrt{\\epsilon}} \\rho,\n$$\nwhere $\\rho = v/\\Omega$ is the gyroradius.\n\nFor passing particles, the guiding-center drifts largely average out over a poloidal transit because $v_\\parallel$ does not reverse, and the drift-induced radial excursion per collision is of order the gyroradius,\n$$\n\\Delta_p \\sim \\rho,\n$$\nup to order-unity geometry factors, since there is no bounce-induced coherent radial step of magnitude $\\Delta_b$.\n\nIn neoclassical theory, the collisional radial transport can be approximated as a random walk with step size $\\Delta$ and step rate $\\nu$:\n$$\nD \\sim \\nu \\Delta^2.\n$$\nThis scaling captures the idea that each pitch-angle-scattering event changes the orbit class or phase sufficiently to decorrelate the radial position and generate a new step of size $\\Delta$. In the banana regime, defined by\n$$\n\\nu_* = \\frac{\\nu q R}{v} \\ll 1,\n$$\nthe bounce motion is much faster than the collisions,\n$$\n\\omega_b \\gg \\nu,\n$$\nso trapped particles complete many bounces between collisions and coherently explore their banana orbits. The characteristic step for trapped particles is the banana width $\\Delta_b$, and since only the trapped subset contributes this way, the effective diffusivity due to trapped particles scales as\n$$\nD_t \\sim \\nu f_t \\Delta_b^2 \\sim \\nu \\left(\\sqrt{\\epsilon}\\right) \\left(\\frac{q^2}{\\epsilon}\\rho^2\\right) = \\nu \\left(\\frac{q^2}{\\sqrt{\\epsilon}}\\right)\\rho^2.\n$$\nThe passing-particle diffusivity scales as\n$$\nD_p \\sim \\nu \\Delta_p^2 \\sim \\nu \\rho^2.\n$$\nTherefore, their ratio is\n$$\n\\frac{D_t}{D_p} \\sim \\frac{q^2}{\\sqrt{\\epsilon}} \\gg 1,\n$$\nfor typical tokamak parameters $q \\gtrsim \\mathcal{O}(1)$ and $\\epsilon \\ll 1$. This shows that trapped particles dominate neoclassical transport in the banana regime, even though they are a fraction $f_t \\sim \\sqrt{\\epsilon}$ of the population, because each trapped particle executes much larger radial excursions than a passing particle.\n\nWe now analyze each option:\n\nA. This option correctly uses the condition $\\nu_* \\ll 1$ to assert $\\omega_b \\gg \\nu$, ensuring that trapped particles maintain banana orbits over many bounces. It derives the banana width scaling:\n$$\n\\Delta_b \\sim \\frac{v_d}{\\omega_b} \\sim \\frac{v}{\\Omega}\\frac{q}{\\sqrt{\\epsilon}} = \\frac{q}{\\sqrt{\\epsilon}}\\rho,\n$$\nand combines the trapped fraction $f_t \\sim \\sqrt{\\epsilon}$ with the random-walk estimate $D_t \\sim \\nu f_t \\Delta_b^2$ to obtain\n$$\nD_t \\sim \\nu \\left(\\frac{q^2}{\\sqrt{\\epsilon}}\\right)\\rho^2,\n$$\nwhich is larger than $D_p \\sim \\nu \\rho^2$ by the factor $q^2/\\sqrt{\\epsilon} \\gg 1$. This matches the principle-based derivation and explains the dominance of trapped particles. Verdict: Correct.\n\nB. This option incorrectly states $\\Delta_p \\sim R$ for passing particles. The guiding-center orbit excursion for passing particles is not of order the major radius; it is of order the gyroradius up to geometry factors, $\\Delta_p \\sim \\rho$, because drifts largely average over a poloidal circuit without creating a macroscopic radial step of order $R$. Thus $D_p \\sim \\nu R^2$ is unphysical and vastly overestimates passing transport. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nC. This option misinterprets the banana regime. Although $\\nu_* \\ll 1$ implies collisions are infrequent compared to bounce motion, it does not imply the absence of transport. Neoclassical transport in this regime arises because collisions intermittently change the orbit class or pitch, producing random-walk steps of size $\\Delta_b$ for trapped particles. Therefore trapped particles do contribute appreciably, and they dominate due to their large $\\Delta_b$. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nD. This option misreads the $\\nu_* \\ll 1$ condition, asserting $\\omega_b \\ll \\nu$. In fact $\\nu_* \\ll 1$ implies $\\nu \\ll \\omega_b$ (equivalently $\\omega_b \\gg \\nu$), so collisions do not destroy trapped orbits between bounces; trapped particles complete many bounces before a significant collision. Thus the conclusion is opposite to the correct physics. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nTherefore, the single best option is A."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "The user wants a detailed validation of the problem statement, followed by a full derivation and evaluation of the options.\n\n### Problem Validation\n\n**Step 1: Extract Givens**\n\n-   **Tokamak Geometry**: Large-aspect-ratio tokamak, major radius $R$, minor radius $r$, inverse aspect ratio $\\epsilon = r/R \\ll 1$.\n-   **Magnetic Field**: Magnitude varies poloidally as $B(\\theta) \\approx B_0 \\left(1 + \\epsilon \\cos \\theta\\right)$, where $\\theta$ is the poloidal angle and $B_0$ is the magnetic field at the magnetic axis.\n-   **Particle Properties**: Mass $m$, charge $e$, total speed $v$.\n-   **Characteristic Frequencies**: Cyclotron frequency $\\Omega = e B_0/m$, pitch-angle-scattering collision frequency $\\nu$.\n-   **Dimensionless Parameters**: Safety factor $q$, collisionality parameter $\\nu_* = \\nu q R / v$.\n-   **Regime**: The banana regime is defined by $\\nu_* \\ll 1$.\n-   **Foundational Facts (Scalings)**:\n    -   Guiding-center drift velocity: $v_d \\sim v^2/(\\Omega R)$.\n    -   Deeply trapped particle bounce frequency: $\\omega_b \\sim (v/(q R)) \\sqrt{\\epsilon}$.\n    -   Trapped fraction: $f_t \\sim \\sqrt{\\epsilon}$.\n-   **Transport Model**: Neoclassical radial transport is estimated as a collisional random walk with diffusivity $D \\sim \\nu \\Delta^2$, where $\\Delta$ is the characteristic radial excursion.\n\n**Step 2: Validate Using Extracted Givens**\n\n-   **Scientifically Grounded**: The problem is firmly rooted in the established theory of neoclassical transport in magnetically confined fusion plasmas. The magnetic field model, the definitions of physical quantities ($\\Omega$, $\\nu_*$), and the provided scalings for $v_d$, $\\omega_b$, and $f_t$ are standard results for a large-aspect-ratio tokamak. The random walk model for diffusion is a common and physically intuitive heuristic used to derive transport scalings. All premises are factually sound within this standard theoretical framework.\n-   **Well-Posed**: The problem asks for a physical explanation based on a given set of scalings. It requires applying these scalings to derive and compare diffusion coefficients for different particle classes (trapped vs. passing). The information provided is sufficient to construct a logical argument and evaluate the options. The question is structured to have a single best answer based on the provided \"foundational facts\".\n-   **Objective**: The problem is stated using precise, objective, and standard terminology from plasma physics. It contains no subjective or ambiguous language.\n\n**Flaw Checklist Assessment**:\n1.  **Scientific/Factual Unsoundness**: None. While a more rigorous definition of the banana regime often involves $\\nu_* / \\epsilon^{3/2} \\ll 1$, the provided definition of $\\nu_* \\ll 1$ is a common simplification and serves as a valid premise for this self-contained problem. The task is to reason *from* the given premises, which are not contradictory.\n4.  **Unrealistic/Infeasible**: The large-aspect-ratio approximation is a standard, physically meaningful limit.\n5.  **Ill-Posed**: A unique, meaningful solution can be derived from the premises.\n6.  **Pseudo-Profound/Trivial**: The problem requires a non-trivial, multi-step scaling analysis that captures the essence of a key concept in transport theory. It is not trivial.\n7.  **Outside Scientific Verifiability**: The claims are entirely verifiable through the application of physics principles and mathematical derivation.\n\n**Step 3: Verdict and Action**\n\nThe problem statement is **valid**. It presents a standard, albeit simplified, model of neoclassical transport and asks for a logical derivation based on it. I will now proceed with the solution.\n\n### Derivation and Option Analysis\n\nThe problem asks to explain why trapped particles dominate neoclassical transport in the banana regime, which is characterized by infrequent collisions. We will estimate the diffusion coefficients for trapped and passing particles using the provided random walk model and scalings. The overall diffusion coefficient is a sum of contributions from different particle populations, weighted by their fraction. The general model is that of a random walk, $D \\sim f \\nu_{\\text{eff}} \\Delta^2$, where $f$ is the particle fraction, $\\nu_{\\text{eff}}$ is the effective collision frequency for a step, and $\\Delta$ is the step size. The problem provides a simplified model $D \\sim \\nu \\Delta^2$ and expects the fraction $f_t$ to be included, as seen in option A.\n\n**1. Transport due to Trapped Particles ($D_t$)**\n\nIn the banana regime, trapped particles execute their characteristic banana-shaped orbits between collisions. The condition for this is that the bounce frequency is much larger than the effective collision frequency, $\\omega_b \\gg \\nu_{\\text{eff}}$. This allows for the formation of coherent orbits.\n\n-   **Step Size ($\\Delta_t$)**: The radial step size for the random walk is the radial width of the banana orbit, $\\Delta_b$. This width is caused by the guiding-center drift velocity, $v_d$, acting over the time it takes to complete a bounce, $T_b \\sim 1/\\omega_b$.\n    $$ \\Delta_t = \\Delta_b \\sim v_d \\cdot T_b \\sim \\frac{v_d}{\\omega_b} $$\n    Using the provided scalings $v_d \\sim v^2/(\\Omega R)$ and $\\omega_b \\sim (v/(q R)) \\sqrt{\\epsilon}$:\n    $$ \\Delta_b \\sim \\frac{v^2 / (\\Omega R)}{(v / (qR)) \\sqrt{\\epsilon}} = \\frac{v^2}{\\Omega R} \\frac{q R}{v \\sqrt{\\epsilon}} = \\frac{v q}{\\Omega \\sqrt{\\epsilon}} $$\n    Introducing the gyroradius $\\rho \\sim v/\\Omega$, the banana width scales as:\n    $$ \\Delta_b \\sim \\frac{q}{\\sqrt{\\epsilon}} \\rho $$\n    Since $q > 1$ and $\\epsilon \\ll 1$, the banana width $\\Delta_b$ is significantly larger than the gyroradius $\\rho$.\n\n-   **Diffusion Coefficient ($D_t$)**: The total transport is proportional to the fraction of particles participating, $f_t$. We use the simplified diffusion model suggested by the problem structure and Option A: $D_t \\sim f_t \\nu \\Delta_t^2$. A collision, occurring with frequency $\\nu$, knocks a particle off its banana orbit, leading to a random step of size $\\Delta_b$.\n    Using the scaling $f_t \\sim \\sqrt{\\epsilon}$:\n    $$ D_t \\sim f_t \\nu \\Delta_b^2 \\sim (\\sqrt{\\epsilon}) \\nu \\left( \\frac{q}{\\sqrt{\\epsilon}} \\rho \\right)^2 = \\sqrt{\\epsilon} \\nu \\frac{q^2 \\rho^2}{\\epsilon} = \\frac{\\nu q^2 \\rho^2}{\\sqrt{\\epsilon}} $$\n\n**2. Transport due to Passing Particles ($D_p$)**\n\nPassing particles are not trapped and circulate toroidally. Their orbits deviate from a magnetic flux surface by approximately one gyroradius.\n\n-   **Step Size ($\\Delta_p$)**: Collisions cause passing particles to random-walk between adjacent flux surfaces. The characteristic step size for this process is the gyroradius.\n    $$ \\Delta_p \\sim \\rho $$\n\n-   **Diffusion Coefficient ($D_p$)**: The fraction of passing particles is $f_p = 1 - f_t \\approx 1$ for $\\epsilon \\ll 1$. The collision frequency is simply $\\nu$.\n    $$ D_p \\sim f_p \\nu \\Delta_p^2 \\sim (1) \\nu \\rho^2 = \\nu \\rho^2 $$\n    This is the scaling for classical diffusion.\n\n**3. Comparison**\n\nNow we compare the diffusion coefficients for trapped and passing particles:\n$$ \\frac{D_t}{D_p} \\sim \\frac{\\nu q^2 \\rho^2 / \\sqrt{\\epsilon}}{\\nu \\rho^2} = \\frac{q^2}{\\sqrt{\\epsilon}} $$\nIn a typical tokamak, $q \\sim 2-4$ and $\\epsilon \\sim 0.1-0.3$. For example, with $q=3$ and $\\epsilon=0.1$, the ratio is $q^2/\\sqrt{\\epsilon} = 9/\\sqrt{0.1} \\approx 9/0.316 \\approx 28.5$. Since $q>1$ and $\\epsilon \\ll 1$ are defining features of a tokamak, the factor $q^2/\\sqrt{\\epsilon}$ is always much greater than $1$.\nTherefore, $D_t \\gg D_p$, and neoclassical transport in the banana regime is dominated by the contribution from trapped particles.\n\n### Evaluation of Options\n\n**A. In the banana regime, collisions are infrequent compared to bounce motion ($\\nu_* \\ll 1$ implies $\\omega_b \\gg \\nu$), so trapped particles maintain coherent banana orbits whose radial excursion per bounce scales as $\\Delta_b \\sim v_d/\\omega_b \\sim (v/\\Omega)(q/\\sqrt{\\epsilon}) = (q/\\sqrt{\\epsilon}) \\rho$, where $\\rho = v/\\Omega$ is the gyroradius. With a trapped fraction $f_t \\sim \\sqrt{\\epsilon}$, the trapped-particle diffusivity scales as $D_t \\sim \\nu f_t \\Delta_b^2 \\sim \\nu \\left(\\sqrt{\\epsilon}\\right) \\left(q^2 \\rho^2/\\epsilon\\right) \\sim \\nu \\left(q^2 \\rho^2/\\sqrt{\\epsilon}\\right)$, which exceeds the passing-particle diffusivity $D_p \\sim \\nu \\rho^2$. Therefore trapped particles dominate.**\n\n-   This option correctly identifies that the banana regime implies $\\omega_b \\gg \\nu$, allowing coherent orbits.\n-   It correctly derives the scaling for the banana width: $\\Delta_b \\sim (q/\\sqrt{\\epsilon})\\rho$.\n-   It correctly constructs the trapped particle diffusivity scaling: $D_t \\sim f_t \\nu \\Delta_b^2 \\sim \\nu q^2 \\rho^2 / \\sqrt{\\epsilon}$.\n-   It correctly identifies the passing particle diffusivity scaling $D_p \\sim \\nu \\rho^2$ for comparison.\n-   It correctly concludes that $D_t \\gg D_p$, explaining the dominance of trapped particles.\n-   The entire chain of reasoning is consistent with our derivation from first principles.\n-   **Verdict: Correct**\n\n**B. Passing particles dominate because they constitute the majority of particles for $\\epsilon \\ll 1$ and their radial excursion per collision is of order the major radius, $\\Delta_p \\sim R$, so $D_p \\sim \\nu R^2 \\gg D_t$.**\n\n-   The statement that passing particles are the majority is correct ($f_p \\approx 1$).\n-   The statement that the radial excursion per collision is of order the major radius, $\\Delta_p \\sim R$, is fundamentally incorrect. The collisional step size for a passing particle is the gyroradius, $\\rho$. The major radius $R$ is orders of magnitude larger than $\\rho$. This premise is physically wrong.\n-   The resulting conclusion is therefore invalid.\n-   **Verdict: Incorrect**\n\n**C. Trapped particles are effectively collisionless when $\\nu_* \\ll 1$, so they do not contribute to transport; only passing particles, whose collisions are more frequent, contribute appreciably to neoclassical transport.**\n\n-   The premise that trapped particles are \"effectively collisionless\" (meaning they complete orbits before scattering) is correct.\n-   The conclusion that they \"do not contribute to transport\" is incorrect. Transport is a random walk process, which requires collisions. Being collisionless simply allows the step size of the random walk ($\\Delta_b$) to become very large. It is the infrequent collisions acting on these large orbits that drive the enhanced transport. If there were zero collisions, the particles would be perfectly confined (in an ideal axisymmetric system).\n-   **Verdict: Incorrect**\n\n**D. The banana regime condition $\\nu_* \\ll 1$ implies $\\omega_b \\ll \\nu$, so collisions destroy trapped orbits before a bounce completes, suppressing trapped-particle transport and enhancing passing-particle dominance.**\n\n-   The statement \"$\\nu_* \\ll 1$ implies $\\omega_b \\ll \\nu$\" is the exact opposite of the physical condition for the banana regime. The banana regime is defined by particles completing their bounces, which requires the bounce frequency to be much *greater* than the collision frequency ($\\omega_b \\gg \\nu$). The condition $\\omega_b \\ll \\nu$ describes the highly collisional Pfirsch-Schlüter regime.\n-   The initial premise is factually wrong, so the entire argument is invalid.\n-   **Verdict: Incorrect**"}]


title: Calculating the leading-order external magnetic multipole moment generated solely by the Pfirsch-Schlüter current in a large-aspect-ratio, circular tokamak

Question: In a toroidal plasma confinement device like a tokamak, pressure gradients in the curved magnetic field lead to the formation of Pfirsch-Schlüter currents. These currents flow along the magnetic field lines and are necessary to ensure that the total current density vector `j` is divergence-free, i.e., `∇ ⋅ j = 0`. Due to the toroidicity, the main magnetic field `B` is stronger on the inboard side of the torus (small major radius `R`) and weaker on the outboard side (large `R`). This spatial variation causes the Pfirsch-Schlüter current to have a non-uniform poloidal distribution, which in turn can generate a magnetic field that extends outside the plasma.

Consider a simple model of a tokamak with a large aspect ratio (`ε = a/R₀ ≪ 1`) and a circular plasma cross-section of minor radius `a`. The major radius of the torus is `R₀`. The plasma is characterized by a central pressure `p₀` and a parabolic pressure profile given by `p(r) = p₀(1 - (r/a)²)`, where `r` is the minor radial coordinate.

The confining magnetic field is primarily in the toroidal direction, with a magnitude `B₀` on the magnetic axis (`r=0`). The geometry of the magnetic field lines is described by the safety factor, `q`, which is assumed to be constant for this problem. In the large aspect ratio limit, the parallel Pfirsch-Schlüter current density `j_{PS}` is well-approximated by:
$$
j_{PS} = - \frac{2q}{B_0} \frac{dp}{dr} \cos\theta
$$
where `θ` is the poloidal angle, with `θ=0` corresponding to the outboard midplane of the torus.

Assuming that the magnetic field lines are nearly toroidal, the toroidal component of the current density vector `j_φ` can be approximated by the parallel current density, `j_φ ≈ j_{PS}`.

Derive a closed-form expression for the magnitude of the net magnetic dipole moment, `m`, generated by this Pfirsch-Schlüter current distribution.



Answer: [{"answer": "$$\n\\boxed{\\frac{2 \\pi^2 q p_0 R_0 a^2}{B_0}}\n$$", "solution": "The magnetic dipole moment `m` of a current distribution `j(r')` within a volume `V` is given by the integral:\n$$\nm = \\frac{1}{2} \\int_V r' \\times j(r') dV'\n$$\nwhere `r'` is the position vector from the origin (the center of the torus) to the volume element `dV'`.\n\nWe will use a standard toroidal coordinate system `(r, θ, φ)`, where `r` is the minor radius, `θ` is the poloidal angle, and `φ` is the toroidal angle. A point in space is described by the position vector `r'` in a fixed Cartesian system `(x, y, z)` with origin at the center of the torus and `z` along the axis of symmetry:\n$$\nr' = R' \\cos\\varphi' \\mathbf{\\hat{x}} + R' \\sin\\varphi' \\mathbf{\\hat{y}} + Z' \\mathbf{\\hat{z}}\n$$\nwhere `R' = R₀ + r' \\cos\\theta'` is the major radial position and `Z' = r' \\sin\\theta'` is the vertical position.\n\nThe current density is given as purely toroidal: `j(r') = j_φ \\mathbf{\\hat{\\varphi}}`. The unit vector `\\mathbf{\\hat{\\varphi}}` in Cartesian coordinates is `\\mathbf{\\hat{\\varphi}} = -\\sin\\varphi' \\mathbf{\\hat{x}} + \\cos\\varphi' \\mathbf{\\hat{y}}`.\n\nThe integrand `r' \\times j(r')` is:\n$$\nr' \\times j(r') = ( (R_0 + r' \\cos\\theta') \\mathbf{\\hat{R}} + r' \\sin\\theta' \\mathbf{\\hat{z}} ) \\times (j_\\varphi \\mathbf{\\hat{\\varphi}})\n$$\nwhere `\\mathbf{\\hat{R}} = \\cos\\varphi' \\mathbf{\\hat{x}} + \\sin\\varphi' \\mathbf{\\hat{y}}`.\nThe cross products of the unit vectors are `\\mathbf{\\hat{R}} \\times \\mathbf{\\hat{\\varphi}} = \\mathbf{\\hat{z}}` and `\\mathbf{\\hat{z}} \\times \\mathbf{\\hat{\\varphi}} = -\\mathbf{\\hat{R}}`.\nSo, `r' \\times j(r') = j_\\varphi (R_0 + r' \\cos\\theta') \\mathbf{\\hat{z}} - j_\\varphi (r' \\sin\\theta') \\mathbf{\\hat{R}}`.\n\nWe need to integrate this over the plasma volume `V`. The volume element in toroidal coordinates is `dV' = (R_0 + r' \\cos\\theta') r' dr' d\\theta' d\\varphi'`.\n$$\nm = \\frac{1}{2} \\int_0^{2\\pi} d\\varphi' \\int_0^a r' dr' \\int_0^{2\\pi} d\\theta' (R_0 + r' \\cos\\theta') [j_\\varphi (R_0 + r' \\cos\\theta') \\mathbf{\\hat{z}} - j_\\varphi (r' \\sin\\theta') \\mathbf{\\hat{R}}]\n$$\nThe integral of `\\mathbf{\\hat{R}} = \\cos\\varphi' \\mathbf{\\hat{x}} + \\sin\\varphi' \\mathbf{\\hat{y}}` over `\\varphi'` from `0` to `2\\pi` is zero. Therefore, the second term in the square brackets does not contribute to the net dipole moment. The dipole moment is purely in the `\\mathbf{\\hat{z}}` direction.\n$$\nm = \\mathbf{\\hat{z}} \\frac{1}{2} \\int_0^{2\\pi} d\\varphi' \\int_0^a r' dr' \\int_0^{2\\pi} d\\theta' (R_0 + r' \\cos\\theta')^2 j_\\varphi\n$$\nThe integral over `d\\varphi'` gives a factor of `2\\pi`:\n$$\nm = \\mathbf{\\hat{z}} \\pi \\int_0^a r' dr' \\int_0^{2\\pi} d\\theta' (R_0 + r' \\cos\\theta')^2 j_\\varphi\n$$\nNext, we substitute the expression for `j_\\varphi ≈ j_{PS}`. First, we find `dp/dr` from the given pressure profile `p(r) = p₀(1 - (r/a)²)`:\n$$\n\\frac{dp}{dr} = p_0 \\frac{d}{dr}(1 - \\frac{r^2}{a^2}) = - \\frac{2 p_0 r}{a^2}\n$$\nSubstituting this into the expression for `j_{PS}`:\n$$\nj_\\varphi = - \\frac{2q}{B_0} \\left(- \\frac{2 p_0 r'}{a^2}\\right) \\cos\\theta' = \\frac{4 q p_0 r'}{B_0 a^2} \\cos\\theta'\n$$\nNow, substitute this `j_\\varphi` into the integral for `m`:\n$$\nm_z = \\pi \\int_0^a r' dr' \\int_0^{2\\pi} d\\theta' (R_0^2 + 2 R_0 r' \\cos\\theta' + r'^2 \\cos^2\\theta') \\left( \\frac{4 q p_0 r'}{B_0 a^2} \\cos\\theta' \\right)\n$$\nWe can separate the integrals over `r'` and `\\theta'`:\n$$\nm_z = \\frac{4 \\pi q p_0}{B_0 a^2} \\int_0^a r'^2 dr' \\int_0^{2\\pi} d\\theta' (R_0^2 \\cos\\theta' + 2 R_0 r' \\cos^2\\theta' + r'^2 \\cos^3\\theta')\n$$\nWe evaluate the integral over `\\theta'`:\n`\\int_0^{2\\pi} \\cos\\theta' d\\theta' = 0`\n`\\int_0^{2\\pi} \\cos^2\\theta' d\\theta' = \\int_0^{2\\pi} \\frac{1+\\cos(2\\theta')}{2} d\\theta' = \\pi`\n`\\int_0^{2\\pi} \\cos^3\\theta' d\\theta' = 0`\nThus, only the middle term of the `\\theta'` integral is non-zero:\n$$\n\\int_0^{2\\pi} (...) d\\theta' = 2 R_0 r' (\\pi) = 2\\pi R_0 r'\n$$\nSubstituting this back into the expression for `m_z`:\n$$\nm_z = \\frac{4 \\pi q p_0}{B_0 a^2} \\int_0^a r'^2 (2\\pi R_0 r') dr' = \\frac{8 \\pi^2 q p_0 R_0}{B_0 a^2} \\int_0^a r'^3 dr'\n$$\nFinally, we perform the radial integration:\n$$\n\\int_0^a r'^3 dr' = \\left[ \\frac{r'^4}{4} \\right]_0^a = \\frac{a^4}{4}\n$$\nSo, the vertical component of the magnetic dipole moment is:\n$$\nm_z = \\frac{8 \\pi^2 q p_0 R_0}{B_0 a^2} \\left( \\frac{a^4}{4} \\right) = \\frac{2 \\pi^2 q p_0 R_0 a^2}{B_0}\n$$\nThe magnitude of the magnetic dipole moment `m` is simply `|m_z|`, assuming `p_0 > 0`.\n$$\nm = \\frac{2 \\pi^2 q p_0 R_0 a^2}{B_0}\n$$"}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{\\frac{2\\pi^2q\\,p_0R_0a^2}{B_0}}$$", "solution": "We begin with the given pressure profile and its radial derivative.  \n$$p(r)=p_0\\Bigl(1-\\frac{r^2}{a^2}\\Bigr)\\quad\\Longrightarrow\\quad\n\\frac{dp}{dr}=-\\frac{2p_0\\,r}{a^2}\\,. $$  \nHence the Pfirsch–Schlüter current density is  \n$$j_{PS}\n=-\\frac{2q}{B_0}\\frac{dp}{dr}\\cos\\theta\n=\\frac{4q\\,p_0}{B_0\\,a^2}\\,r\\cos\\theta\\,. $$  \nWe approximate the toroidal current density by $j_\\phi\\approx j_{PS}$ and compute the magnetic dipole moment  \n$$\\mathbf m=\\frac12\\int\\mathbf r\\times\\mathbf j\\,dV\\,. $$  \nIn cylindrical coordinates $(R,\\phi,Z)$ one finds the only non-vanishing component  \n$$m_z\n=\\frac12\\!\\int R\\,j_\\phi\\,dV\n=\\frac12\\int_0^{2\\pi}\\!d\\phi\\int_0^a\\!dr\\int_0^{2\\pi}\\!d\\theta\\;R^2\\,j_{PS}\\,r\\,. $$  \nWith $R=R_0+r\\cos\\theta\\approx R_0+r\\cos\\theta$ and keeping terms to first order in $r/R_0$,  \n$$R^2\\approx R_0^2+2R_0r\\cos\\theta\\,, $$  \nso that  \n\\begin{align*}\nm_z&=\\frac12\\,(2\\pi)\\int_0^a dr\\int_0^{2\\pi}d\\theta\\,\n\\bigl(R_0^2+2R_0r\\cos\\theta\\bigr)\\frac{4q\\,p_0}{B_0\\,a^2}\\,r\\cos\\theta\\;r\\\\\n&=\\frac{4\\pi q\\,p_0}{B_0a^2}\\int_0^adr\\Bigl[ \nR_0^2r^2\\!\\int_0^{2\\pi}\\cos\\theta\\,d\\theta\n+2R_0r^3\\!\\int_0^{2\\pi}\\cos^2\\theta\\,d\\theta\\Bigr]\\\\\n&=\\frac{4\\pi q\\,p_0}{B_0a^2}\\,2\\pi R_0\\int_0^a r^3dr\n=\\frac{8\\pi^2q\\,p_0R_0}{B_0a^2}\\,\\frac{a^4}{4}\n=\\frac{2\\pi^2q\\,p_0R_0a^2}{B_0}\\,.\n\\end{align*}\nThus the magnitude of the magnetic dipole moment is\n$$m=\\frac{2\\pi^2q\\,p_0R_0a^2}{B_0}\\,. $$"}]


title: [calculation] Show how collisional diffusion $D$ competes with the Ware convection $V$ and derive the steady-state solution $n(\psi)$ for constant $D$ and $V$.

Question: Consider a toroidally confined plasma with nested, axisymmetric flux surfaces labeled by the poloidal magnetic flux coordinate $\psi$, which increases monotonically from the magnetic axis toward the plasma edge. Assume the flux-surface averaged particle density $n(\psi,t)$ evolves according to the continuity law and that the neoclassical inward particle transport associated with the Ware pinch can be represented as a convective contribution to the net radial particle flux. In the absence of volumetric sources and sinks within an annular region $\psi \in [\psi_{0},\psi_{a}]$, steady state is attained when the divergence of the flux-surface averaged particle flux vanishes. In this regime, model the net radial particle flux by a superposition of a collisional diffusive term and a Ware convective term, with a flux-surface averaged diffusion coefficient $D$ and a Ware convection velocity $V$, both taken to be constants in $\psi$ over the interval of interest. Assume further that the flux-surface volume derivative $V'(\psi)$ is approximately constant across this interval, so that the flux-surface averaged radial particle flux is uniform in $\psi$ at steady state.

Starting from mass conservation and these modeling assumptions, derive the steady-state density profile $n(\psi)$ on $\psi \in [\psi_{0},\psi_{a}]$ when the flux-surface averaged radial particle flux is equal to a constant $\Gamma_{0}$ set by global particle balance. Impose the boundary condition $n(\psi_{a})=n_{a}$, where $n_{a}$ is the edge density, and express the final answer as a single closed-form analytic expression in terms of $D$, $V$, $\Gamma_{0}$, $n_{a}$, $\psi$, and $\psi_{a}$. No numerical values are required, and you should not introduce any additional parameters. The final answer must be a single expression, not an equation involving $n(\psi)$ on both sides.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V}+\\left(n_{a}-\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V}\\right)\\exp\\!\\left(\\frac{V}{D}\\,(\\psi-\\psi_{a})\\right)}$$", "solution": "The appropriate starting point is the flux-surface averaged continuity equation for the particle density $n(\\psi,t)$,\n$$\n\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial t}+\\frac{1}{V'(\\psi)}\\frac{\\partial}{\\partial \\psi}\\!\\left[V'(\\psi)\\,\\Gamma(\\psi)\\right]=S(\\psi,t),\n$$\nwhere $V'(\\psi)$ is the derivative of the volume enclosed by the flux surface with respect to $\\psi$, $\\Gamma(\\psi)$ is the flux-surface averaged radial particle flux across the surface labeled by $\\psi$, and $S(\\psi,t)$ is the flux-surface averaged particle source. In the steady state without sources or sinks within the interval $\\psi\\in[\\psi_{0},\\psi_{a}]$, we have\n$$\n\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial t}=0,\\qquad S(\\psi,t)=0,\n$$\nso the continuity equation reduces to\n$$\n\\frac{d}{d\\psi}\\!\\left[V'(\\psi)\\,\\Gamma(\\psi)\\right]=0.\n$$\nIt follows that $V'(\\psi)\\,\\Gamma(\\psi)=C_{0}$, where $C_{0}$ is a constant. Under the stated assumption that $V'(\\psi)$ is approximately constant in the interval of interest, the flux-surface averaged radial particle flux is uniform,\n$$\n\\Gamma(\\psi)=\\Gamma_{0},\n$$\nwith $\\Gamma_{0}=C_{0}/V'$, a constant set by global particle balance and edge conditions.\n\nNext, we adopt the standard decomposition of the flux-surface averaged radial particle flux into a collisional diffusive part and a convective part associated with the Ware pinch. In this model,\n$$\n\\Gamma(\\psi)=-D\\,\\frac{d n}{d\\psi}+V\\,n(\\psi),\n$$\nwhere $D$ is the flux-surface averaged collisional diffusion coefficient and $V$ is the flux-surface averaged Ware convection velocity. Both $D$ and $V$ are taken to be constant in $\\psi$ over the interval considered.\n\nCombining the uniformity of $\\Gamma(\\psi)$ with the transport model yields a first-order linear ordinary differential equation for $n(\\psi)$,\n$$\n-D\\,\\frac{d n}{d\\psi}+V\\,n(\\psi)=\\Gamma_{0}.\n$$\nWe rewrite this in standard form,\n$$\n\\frac{d n}{d\\psi}-\\frac{V}{D}\\,n(\\psi)=-\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{D}.\n$$\nTo solve, we use an integrating factor. The integrating factor is\n$$\n\\mu(\\psi)=\\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\,\\psi\\right).\n$$\nMultiplying the differential equation by $\\mu(\\psi)$ gives\n$$\n\\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\,\\psi\\right)\\frac{d n}{d\\psi}-\\frac{V}{D}\\,\\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\,\\psi\\right)n(\\psi)=-\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{D}\\,\\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\,\\psi\\right),\n$$\nwhich can be recognized as\n$$\n\\frac{d}{d\\psi}\\!\\left[n(\\psi)\\,\\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\,\\psi\\right)\\right]=-\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{D}\\,\\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\,\\psi\\right).\n$$\nIntegrating with respect to $\\psi$ yields\n$$\nn(\\psi)\\,\\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\,\\psi\\right)=\\int -\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{D}\\,\\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\,\\psi\\right)\\,d\\psi + C,\n$$\nwhere $C$ is an integration constant. Evaluating the integral,\n$$\n\\int -\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{D}\\,\\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\,\\psi\\right)\\,d\\psi\n=-\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{D}\\,\\left(-\\frac{D}{V}\\right)\\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\,\\psi\\right)\n=\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V}\\,\\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\,\\psi\\right).\n$$\nTherefore,\n$$\nn(\\psi)\\,\\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\,\\psi\\right)=\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V}\\,\\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\,\\psi\\right)+C,\n$$\nand thus\n$$\nn(\\psi)=\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V}+C\\,\\exp\\!\\left(\\frac{V}{D}\\,\\psi\\right).\n$$\nWe determine $C$ from the boundary condition $n(\\psi_{a})=n_{a}$:\n$$\nn_{a}=\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V}+C\\,\\exp\\!\\left(\\frac{V}{D}\\,\\psi_{a}\\right)\\quad\\Rightarrow\\quad\nC=\\left(n_{a}-\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V}\\right)\\exp\\!\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\,\\psi_{a}\\right).\n$$\nSubstituting $C$ back into the solution gives the steady-state density profile,\n$$\nn(\\psi)=\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V}+\\left(n_{a}-\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V}\\right)\\exp\\!\\left(\\frac{V}{D}\\,(\\psi-\\psi_{a})\\right).\n$$\n\nThis closed-form solution encapsulates the competition between collisional diffusion and Ware convection. The dimensionless ratio $V/D$ sets the characteristic gradient scale length $L=D/V$; for $\\Gamma_{0}=0$, the profile reduces to\n$$\nn(\\psi)=n_{a}\\,\\exp\\!\\left(\\frac{V}{D}\\,(\\psi-\\psi_{a})\\right),\n$$\ndemonstrating that finite Ware convection produces an exponential profile with scale length $D/V$, while in the limit $V\\to 0$ the solution becomes the purely diffusive linear profile\n$$\nn(\\psi)=n_{a}+\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{D}\\,(\\psi-\\psi_{a}).\n$$\nThe sign of $V$ sets whether convection drives inward or outward peaking relative to the outward-increasing $\\psi$ coordinate, and the magnitude of $|V|/D$ controls the strength of the neoclassical inward pinch relative to collisional flattening."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V} + \\left(n_{a} - \\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V}\\right) \\exp\\left(\\frac{V}{D}(\\psi - \\psi_{a})\\right)}$$", "solution": "The user wants me to solve a problem based on neoclassical transport in a tokamak plasma.\n\n### Step 1: Extract Givens\n- **System**: Toroidally confined plasma with nested, axisymmetric flux surfaces labeled by the poloidal magnetic flux coordinate $\\psi$.\n- **Region of Interest**: An annular region defined by $\\psi \\in [\\psi_{0},\\psi_{a}]$.\n- **State**: Steady state, meaning time derivatives of macroscopic quantities are zero.\n- **Sources/Sinks**: Assumed to be absent within the specified annular region.\n- **Particle Flux Model**: The net flux-surface averaged radial particle flux, denoted here as $\\Gamma$, is a superposition of a collisional diffusive term and a Ware convective term.\n- **Model Parameters**:\n    - The flux-surface averaged diffusion coefficient, $D$, is constant over the interval $[\\psi_{0},\\psi_{a}]$.\n    - The flux-surface averaged Ware convection velocity, $V$, is constant over the interval $[\\psi_{0},\\psi_{a}]$.\n- **Steady-State Condition**: As a consequence of mass conservation in a source-free region at steady state, and the assumption of a near-constant flux-surface volume derivative $V'(\\psi)$, the net radial particle flux is uniform in $\\psi$. It is given as a constant, $\\Gamma = \\Gamma_{0}$.\n- **Boundary Condition**: The particle density at the outer boundary of the region is specified as $n(\\psi_{a}) = n_{a}$.\n- **Objective**: Derive the steady-state density profile $n(\\psi)$ on the interval $\\psi \\in [\\psi_{0},\\psi_{a}]$ as a single closed-form expression in terms of the given parameters.\n\n### Step 2: Validate Using Extracted Givens\nThe problem statement is scientifically grounded in the field of plasma physics, specifically neoclassical transport theory. The model described is a standard simplified representation of particle transport in a tokamak, balancing outward Fickian diffusion with the inward Ware pinch. The governing principles (particle conservation) and the mathematical formulation (a first-order ordinary differential equation) are standard.\n\nThe problem is well-posed. It provides a first-order linear ordinary differential equation and one boundary condition, which is mathematically sufficient to determine a unique solution for the density profile $n(\\psi)$. All terms ($D$, $V$, $\\Gamma_{0}$, $n_{a}$, $\\psi_{a}$) are clearly defined as parameters or constants for the purpose of the derivation. The assumptions of constant coefficients ($D$, $V$) are physically reasonable approximations for a narrow radial region and simplify the problem to one with an analytical solution, which is a common pedagogical approach. There are no contradictions, ambiguities, or scientifically unsound claims. The problem is valid.\n\n### Step 3: Derivation of the Solution\nThe starting point is the model for the flux-surface averaged radial particle flux, $\\Gamma$. This flux is composed of a diffusive component and a convective component.\n\nThe diffusive flux is driven by the gradient of the particle density $n$. Following Fick's law, this flux is directed from regions of higher density to lower density. Since the radial coordinate $\\psi$ increases outwards, a negative density gradient $\\frac{dn}{d\\psi} < 0$ (density decreasing outwards) leads to an outward (positive) flux. The diffusive flux is therefore modeled as $-D \\frac{dn}{d\\psi}$.\n\nThe convective flux is due to the Ware pinch, which is an inward drift of trapped particles. This flux is proportional to the local density $n$ and the convection velocity $V$. The term is written as $n V$. For the Ware pinch, the velocity $V$ is directed inwards, so we expect $V < 0$.\n\nThe total net radial particle flux $\\Gamma$ is the sum of these two contributions:\n$$ \\Gamma = -D \\frac{dn}{d\\psi} + nV $$\nAccording to the problem statement, in the source-free, steady-state regime of interest, this flux is constant and equal to $\\Gamma_{0}$. This yields the following first-order linear ordinary differential equation (ODE) for the density profile $n(\\psi)$:\n$$ -D \\frac{dn}{d\\psi} + nV = \\Gamma_{0} $$\nTo solve this equation, we rearrange it into standard form, $y' + P(x)y = Q(x)$:\n$$ \\frac{dn}{d\\psi} - \\frac{V}{D} n(\\psi) = -\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{D} $$\nHere, the independent variable is $\\psi$, the dependent variable is $n(\\psi)$, and the coefficients $P(\\psi) = -\\frac{V}{D}$ and $Q(\\psi) = -\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{D}$ are constants, as $V$, $D$, and $\\Gamma_{0}$ are all stipulated to be constant.\n\nThis ODE can be solved using an integrating factor, $I(\\psi)$, defined as:\n$$ I(\\psi) = \\exp\\left( \\int P(\\psi) \\,d\\psi \\right) = \\exp\\left( \\int -\\frac{V}{D} \\,d\\psi \\right) = \\exp\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\psi\\right) $$\nMultiplying the standard form ODE by the integrating factor $I(\\psi)$:\n$$ \\exp\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\psi\\right) \\frac{dn}{d\\psi} - \\frac{V}{D} \\exp\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\psi\\right) n(\\psi) = -\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{D} \\exp\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\psi\\right) $$\nThe left side of the equation is now the exact derivative of the product $n(\\psi)I(\\psi)$:\n$$ \\frac{d}{d\\psi} \\left[ n(\\psi) \\exp\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\psi\\right) \\right] = -\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{D} \\exp\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\psi\\right) $$\nWe integrate both sides with respect to $\\psi$:\n$$ \\int \\frac{d}{d\\psi} \\left[ n(\\psi) \\exp\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\psi\\right) \\right] d\\psi = \\int -\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{D} \\exp\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\psi\\right) d\\psi $$\n$$ n(\\psi) \\exp\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\psi\\right) = -\\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{D} \\left( \\frac{1}{-V/D} \\exp\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\psi\\right) \\right) + C $$\nwhere $C$ is the constant of integration. Simplifying the right side gives:\n$$ n(\\psi) \\exp\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\psi\\right) = \\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V} \\exp\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\psi\\right) + C $$\nTo find the general solution for $n(\\psi)$, we multiply the entire equation by $\\exp\\left(\\frac{V}{D}\\psi\\right)$:\n$$ n(\\psi) = \\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V} + C \\exp\\left(\\frac{V}{D}\\psi\\right) $$\nThis is the general solution to the ODE. The constant $C$ is determined by applying the boundary condition $n(\\psi_{a}) = n_{a}$. We substitute $\\psi = \\psi_a$ and $n(\\psi_a) = n_a$ into the general solution:\n$$ n_{a} = \\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V} + C \\exp\\left(\\frac{V}{D}\\psi_{a}\\right) $$\nSolving for the constant $C$:\n$$ C \\exp\\left(\\frac{V}{D}\\psi_{a}\\right) = n_{a} - \\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V} $$\n$$ C = \\left( n_{a} - \\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V} \\right) \\exp\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\psi_{a}\\right) $$\nFinally, we substitute this expression for $C$ back into the general solution for $n(\\psi)$:\n$$ n(\\psi) = \\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V} + \\left( \\left( n_{a} - \\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V} \\right) \\exp\\left(-\\frac{V}{D}\\psi_{a}\\right) \\right) \\exp\\left(\\frac{V}{D}\\psi\\right) $$\nCombining the exponential terms gives the final expression for the steady-state density profile:\n$$ n(\\psi) = \\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V} + \\left( n_{a} - \\frac{\\Gamma_{0}}{V} \\right) \\exp\\left(\\frac{V}{D}(\\psi - \\psi_{a})\\right) $$\nThis expression represents the particle density $n$ at any flux surface coordinate $\\psi$ within the specified region, determined by the balance between outward diffusion and inward convection, subject to the given net flux and boundary density."}]


title: [calculation] Evaluate the modification of neoclassical bootstrap current due to turbulent particle transport using a quasilinear framework for fluxes.

Question: Consider a large–aspect-ratio, axisymmetric tokamak plasma in the low-collisionality (banana) regime at a fixed minor radius $r^{\star}$. Assume steady state and employ flux-surface averaging (FSA). Start from the flux-surface-averaged particle continuity equation and a linear quasilinear (QL) response for the turbulent particle flux. At $r^{\star}$, assume there is negligible local particle source, so the total radial particle flux vanishes. Model the total flux as the sum of a neoclassical contribution and a quasilinear turbulent contribution, each linear in the local gradients: the neoclassical particle flux is $-D_{\mathrm{NC}}\,\partial n/\partial r + V_{\mathrm{NC}}\,n$ and the quasilinear turbulent particle flux is $-D_{\mathrm{QL}}\,\partial n/\partial r + V_{p}\,n$, with the radial coordinate $r$ increasing outward and the flux convention taken positive outward. The species are singly charged, and quasi-neutrality $n_{e}=n_{i}\equiv n$ holds.

At $r^{\star}$, take the following measured profiles and transport coefficients:
- Density $n(r^{\star}) = 6.0 \times 10^{19}\,\mathrm{m}^{-3}$.
- Electron temperature $T_{e}(r^{\star}) = 2.5\,\mathrm{keV}$ and ion temperature $T_{i}(r^{\star}) = 3.0\,\mathrm{keV}$.
- Electron temperature gradient $\partial T_{e}/\partial r\big|_{r^{\star}} = -3.0\,\mathrm{keV}\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}$ and ion temperature gradient $\partial T_{i}/\partial r\big|_{r^{\star}} = -2.0\,\mathrm{keV}\,\mathrm{m}^{-1}$.
- Neoclassical diffusivity $D_{\mathrm{NC}} = 0.05\,\mathrm{m}^{2}\,\mathrm{s}^{-1}$ and neoclassical convective velocity $V_{\mathrm{NC}} = -0.010\,\mathrm{m}\,\mathrm{s}^{-1}$.
- Quasilinear turbulent diffusivity $D_{\mathrm{QL}} = 0.50\,\mathrm{m}^{2}\,\mathrm{s}^{-1}$ and turbulent convective (pinch) velocity $V_{p} = -0.050\,\mathrm{m}\,\mathrm{s}^{-1}$.

Treat the pressure as $p = n\,e\,(T_{e}+T_{i})$ when $T_{e}$ and $T_{i}$ are expressed in electronvolts, where $e$ is the elementary charge. The elementary charge is $e = 1.602176634 \times 10^{-19}\,\mathrm{C}$. In the banana regime, the neoclassical closure for the bootstrap current density relates the parallel current to the pressure gradient through a geometry- and collisionality-dependent proportionality at $r^{\star}$. A machine-dependent coefficient extracted independently from neoclassical analysis at $r^{\star}$ is provided: $K = 0.35\,\mathrm{A}\,(\mathrm{Pa}\,\mathrm{m})^{-1}$.

Using a derivation that begins with the drift-kinetic equation (DKE) and the flux-surface-averaged parallel momentum balance to motivate the bootstrap current’s dependence on pressure gradient, and using the quasilinear framework for the turbulent particle flux given above, evaluate the modification of the neoclassical bootstrap current density due to turbulent particle transport at $r^{\star}$, defined as $\Delta j_{\mathrm{bs}} \equiv j_{\mathrm{bs}}^{\mathrm{NC+QL}} - j_{\mathrm{bs}}^{\mathrm{NC}}$. Express your final result in $\mathrm{A}\,\mathrm{m}^{-2}$ and round your answer to four significant figures.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{-1.682 \\times 10^{3}}$$", "solution": "We begin from the flux-surface-averaged (FSA) continuity equation for particles in steady state,\n$$\n\\frac{1}{V'}\\frac{\\partial}{\\partial r}\\!\\left(V'\\,\\Gamma\\right) + S = 0,\n$$\nwhere $V'$ is the derivative of the flux-surface volume with respect to $r$, $\\Gamma$ is the radial particle flux, and $S$ is the net particle source on the flux surface. At the chosen location $r^{\\star}$, the local source is negligible, and in a narrow radial region we take $V'$ slowly varying so that the leading balance is $\\Gamma(r^{\\star}) \\simeq 0$.\n\nThe total radial flux is modeled as the sum of neoclassical and turbulent quasilinear contributions, each linear in the gradient,\n$$\n\\Gamma = \\Gamma_{\\mathrm{NC}} + \\Gamma_{\\mathrm{QL}} = \\left[-D_{\\mathrm{NC}}\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r} + V_{\\mathrm{NC}}\\,n\\right] + \\left[-D_{\\mathrm{QL}}\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r} + V_{p}\\,n\\right].\n$$\nImposing $\\Gamma(r^{\\star}) = 0$ yields the local density gradient with and without turbulence. Without turbulence (neoclassical-only),\n$$\n\\Gamma_{\\mathrm{NC}} = -D_{\\mathrm{NC}}\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r} + V_{\\mathrm{NC}}\\,n = 0\n\\quad\\Rightarrow\\quad\n\\left.\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r}\\right|_{\\mathrm{NC}} = \\frac{V_{\\mathrm{NC}}}{D_{\\mathrm{NC}}}\\,n.\n$$\nWith turbulence (neoclassical plus quasilinear),\n$$\n\\Gamma_{\\mathrm{NC+QL}} = -\\left(D_{\\mathrm{NC}} + D_{\\mathrm{QL}}\\right)\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r} + \\left(V_{\\mathrm{NC}} + V_{p}\\right)\\,n = 0\n\\quad\\Rightarrow\\quad\n\\left.\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r}\\right|_{\\mathrm{NC+QL}} = \\frac{V_{\\mathrm{NC}} + V_{p}}{D_{\\mathrm{NC}} + D_{\\mathrm{QL}}}\\,n.\n$$\n\nTo connect the bootstrap current density to the pressure gradient, we start from the drift-kinetic equation (DKE) for each species’ distribution function $f_{s}$ in an axisymmetric tokamak, linearized about a Maxwellian $f_{s0}$ and including the parallel electric field and toroidal geometry. In the low-collisionality banana regime, the solution for the first-order correction $f_{s1}$ leads to a parallel current driven by the diamagnetic (pressure-gradient) component of the parallel force balance and the requirement of quasineutrality and ambipolarity. The flux-surface-averaged parallel momentum balance, together with the Pfirsch–Schlüter (PS) contribution and trapped-particle dynamics, yields a net bootstrap current density proportional to the radial pressure gradient,\n$$\nj_{\\mathrm{bs}} = -K\\,\\frac{\\partial p}{\\partial r},\n$$\nwhere $K$ encapsulates the geometric factors (inverse aspect ratio, safety factor, field pitch), collisionality dependence, and trapped-particle fraction for the given flux surface. The sign convention here takes $r$ outward and $K>0$, so a negative pressure gradient (pressure decreasing outward) drives a positive bootstrap current density.\n\nWe now compute the pressure gradient with and without turbulence. With temperatures specified in electronvolts and using $p = n\\,e\\,(T_{e} + T_{i})$, the total derivative is\n$$\n\\frac{\\partial p}{\\partial r} = e\\left[\\left(T_{e} + T_{i}\\right)\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r} + n\\left(\\frac{\\partial T_{e}}{\\partial r} + \\frac{\\partial T_{i}}{\\partial r}\\right)\\right].\n$$\nIntroduce the given values:\n- $n = 6.0 \\times 10^{19}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-3}$,\n- $T_{e} = 2.5\\,\\mathrm{keV}$, $T_{i} = 3.0\\,\\mathrm{keV}$, so $T_{e}+T_{i} = 5.5\\,\\mathrm{keV} = 5.5 \\times 10^{3}\\,\\mathrm{eV}$,\n- $\\partial T_{e}/\\partial r = -3.0 \\times 10^{3}\\,\\mathrm{eV}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-1}$, $\\partial T_{i}/\\partial r = -2.0 \\times 10^{3}\\,\\mathrm{eV}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-1}$, hence $\\partial(T_{e}+T_{i})/\\partial r = -5.0 \\times 10^{3}\\,\\mathrm{eV}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-1}$,\n- $D_{\\mathrm{NC}} = 0.05\\,\\mathrm{m}^{2}\\,\\mathrm{s}^{-1}$, $V_{\\mathrm{NC}} = -0.010\\,\\mathrm{m}\\,\\mathrm{s}^{-1}$,\n- $D_{\\mathrm{QL}} = 0.50\\,\\mathrm{m}^{2}\\,\\mathrm{s}^{-1}$, $V_{p} = -0.050\\,\\mathrm{m}\\,\\mathrm{s}^{-1}$,\n- $e = 1.602176634 \\times 10^{-19}\\,\\mathrm{C}$.\n\nFirst, compute the neoclassical-only density gradient:\n$$\n\\left.\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r}\\right|_{\\mathrm{NC}} = \\frac{-0.010}{0.05}\\,n = -0.2\\,n = -0.2 \\times \\left(6.0 \\times 10^{19}\\right)\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-4} = -1.2 \\times 10^{19}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-4}.\n$$\nThen the neoclassical-only pressure gradient is\n$$\n\\left.\\frac{\\partial p}{\\partial r}\\right|_{\\mathrm{NC}} \n= e\\left[\\left(5.5 \\times 10^{3}\\right)\\left(-1.2 \\times 10^{19}\\right) + \\left(6.0 \\times 10^{19}\\right)\\left(-5.0 \\times 10^{3}\\right)\\right]\n= e\\left[-6.6 \\times 10^{22} - 3.0 \\times 10^{23}\\right]\\,\\mathrm{eV}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-4}.\n$$\nConvert to $\\mathrm{Pa}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-1}$ using $1\\,\\mathrm{eV} = e\\,\\mathrm{J}$,\n$$\n\\left.\\frac{\\partial p}{\\partial r}\\right|_{\\mathrm{NC}} \n= \\left(1.602176634 \\times 10^{-19}\\right)\\left(-3.66 \\times 10^{23}\\right)\\,\\mathrm{J}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-4}\n= -5.86396648044 \\times 10^{4}\\,\\mathrm{Pa}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-1}.\n$$\n\nNext, include turbulence. The density gradient becomes\n$$\n\\left.\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r}\\right|_{\\mathrm{NC+QL}} \n= \\frac{-0.010 - 0.050}{0.05 + 0.50}\\,n \n= \\frac{-0.060}{0.55}\\,n \n= -0.109090909\\ldots\\,n \n= -0.109090909 \\times \\left(6.0 \\times 10^{19}\\right)\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-4}\n= -6.545454545 \\times 10^{18}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-4}.\n$$\nThe corresponding pressure gradient is\n$$\n\\left.\\frac{\\partial p}{\\partial r}\\right|_{\\mathrm{NC+QL}} \n= e\\left[\\left(5.5 \\times 10^{3}\\right)\\left(-6.545454545 \\times 10^{18}\\right) + \\left(6.0 \\times 10^{19}\\right)\\left(-5.0 \\times 10^{3}\\right)\\right]\n= e\\left[-3.60099999975 \\times 10^{22} - 3.0 \\times 10^{23}\\right]\\,\\mathrm{eV}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-4},\n$$\nwhich evaluates to\n$$\n\\left.\\frac{\\partial p}{\\partial r}\\right|_{\\mathrm{NC+QL}} \n= \\left(1.602176634 \\times 10^{-19}\\right)\\left(-3.36099999975 \\times 10^{23}\\right)\\,\\mathrm{J}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-4}\n= -5.38331349024 \\times 10^{4}\\,\\mathrm{Pa}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-1}.\n$$\n\nWith $j_{\\mathrm{bs}} = -K\\,\\partial p/\\partial r$ and $K = 0.35\\,\\mathrm{A}\\,(\\mathrm{Pa}\\,\\mathrm{m})^{-1}$, the neoclassical-only bootstrap current density is\n$$\nj_{\\mathrm{bs}}^{\\mathrm{NC}} = -0.35 \\times \\left(-5.86396648044 \\times 10^{4}\\right) = 2.05238826815 \\times 10^{4}\\,\\mathrm{A}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-2},\n$$\nand the bootstrap current density with turbulence is\n$$\nj_{\\mathrm{bs}}^{\\mathrm{NC+QL}} = -0.35 \\times \\left(-5.38331349024 \\times 10^{4}\\right) = 1.88415972158 \\times 10^{4}\\,\\mathrm{A}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-2}.\n$$\nTherefore, the modification due to turbulent particle transport is\n$$\n\\Delta j_{\\mathrm{bs}} \\equiv j_{\\mathrm{bs}}^{\\mathrm{NC+QL}} - j_{\\mathrm{bs}}^{\\mathrm{NC}} \n= \\left(1.88415972158 - 2.05238826815\\right) \\times 10^{4}\\,\\mathrm{A}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-2}\n= -1.6822854657 \\times 10^{3}\\,\\mathrm{A}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-2}.\n$$\nRounded to four significant figures and expressed in $\\mathrm{A}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-2}$, the result is $-1.682 \\times 10^{3}\\,\\mathrm{A}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-2}$."}, {"answer": "$$\n\\boxed{-1.682 \\times 10^{3}}\n$$", "solution": "The problem requires the evaluation of the modification of the neoclassical bootstrap current density, $\\Delta j_{\\mathrm{bs}}$, due to the presence of quasilinear turbulent particle transport. This modification arises because turbulence alters the steady-state plasma profiles, which in turn drive the bootstrap current. Specifically, the bootstrap current is driven by the pressure gradient. The pressure gradient depends on the density and temperature gradients. While the temperature profiles are given, the density gradient is determined by the steady-state particle transport balance.\n\nThe total radial particle flux at the minor radius $r^{\\star}$, denoted $\\Gamma_{r}$, is the sum of the neoclassical flux, $\\Gamma_{r}^{\\mathrm{NC}}$, and the quasilinear turbulent flux, $\\Gamma_{r}^{\\mathrm{QL}}$.\n$$\n\\Gamma_{r} = \\Gamma_{r}^{\\mathrm{NC}} + \\Gamma_{r}^{\\mathrm{QL}}\n$$\nThe problem provides linear models for these fluxes:\n$$\n\\Gamma_{r}^{\\mathrm{NC}} = -D_{\\mathrm{NC}}\\,\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r} + V_{\\mathrm{NC}}\\,n\n$$\n$$\n\\Gamma_{r}^{\\mathrm{QL}} = -D_{\\mathrm{QL}}\\,\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r} + V_{p}\\,n\n$$\nIn steady state with a negligible local particle source, the total particle flux must vanish: $\\Gamma_{r}(r^{\\star}) = 0$. This condition determines the actual density gradient at $r^{\\star}$, which we denote as $(\\partial n/\\partial r)_{\\mathrm{total}}$.\n$$\n(-D_{\\mathrm{NC}}\\,\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r} + V_{\\mathrm{NC}}\\,n) + (-D_{\\mathrm{QL}}\\,\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r} + V_{p}\\,n) = 0\n$$\n$$\n-(D_{\\mathrm{NC}} + D_{\\mathrm{QL}})\\left(\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r}\\right)_{\\mathrm{total}} + (V_{\\mathrm{NC}} + V_{p})n = 0\n$$\nSolving for the density gradient gives:\n$$\n\\left(\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r}\\right)_{\\mathrm{total}} = \\frac{(V_{\\mathrm{NC}} + V_{p})n}{D_{\\mathrm{NC}} + D_{\\mathrm{QL}}}\n$$\nThe problem defines the modification to the bootstrap current as $\\Delta j_{\\mathrm{bs}} \\equiv j_{\\mathrm{bs}}^{\\mathrm{NC+QL}} - j_{\\mathrm{bs}}^{\\mathrm{NC}}$. Here, $j_{\\mathrm{bs}}^{\\mathrm{NC+QL}}$ is the current calculated with the actual pressure gradient present in the plasma, which is determined by the total transport. In contrast, $j_{\\mathrm{bs}}^{\\mathrm{NC}}$ is the hypothetical current that would exist if turbulence were absent ($\\Gamma_{r}^{\\mathrm{QL}} = 0$). In this hypothetical neoclassical-only case, the particle balance would be $\\Gamma_{r}^{\\mathrm{NC}} = 0$, leading to a different density gradient, $(\\partial n/\\partial r)_{\\mathrm{NC}}$:\n$$\n-D_{\\mathrm{NC}}\\left(\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r}\\right)_{\\mathrm{NC}} + V_{\\mathrm{NC}}\\,n = 0 \\quad \\implies \\quad \\left(\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r}\\right)_{\\mathrm{NC}} = \\frac{V_{\\mathrm{NC}}\\,n}{D_{\\mathrm{NC}}}\n$$\nThe bootstrap current density is related to the pressure gradient via the coefficient $K$: $j_{\\mathrm{bs}} = -K (\\partial p / \\partial r)$. The pressure $p$ in Pascals is given by $p = n\\,e\\,(T_{e}+T_{i})$, where temperatures are in eV. The pressure gradient is:\n$$\n\\frac{\\partial p}{\\partial r} = e (T_{e}+T_{i})\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r} + e n \\left(\\frac{\\partial T_{e}}{\\partial r} + \\frac{\\partial T_{i}}{\\partial r}\\right)\n$$\nThe modification of the bootstrap current is then:\n$$\n\\Delta j_{\\mathrm{bs}} = -K \\left( \\left(\\frac{\\partial p}{\\partial r}\\right)_{\\mathrm{total}} - \\left(\\frac{\\partial p}{\\partial r}\\right)_{\\mathrm{NC}} \\right)\n$$\nThe temperature profiles are fixed. Therefore, the difference in pressure gradients is solely due to the difference in density gradients:\n$$\n\\left(\\frac{\\partial p}{\\partial r}\\right)_{\\mathrm{total}} - \\left(\\frac{\\partial p}{\\partial r}\\right)_{\\mathrm{NC}} = e (T_{e}+T_{i}) \\left( \\left(\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r}\\right)_{\\mathrm{total}} - \\left(\\frac{\\partial n}{\\partial r}\\right)_{\\mathrm{NC}} \\right)\n$$\nSubstituting the expressions for the density gradients:\n$$\n\\Delta j_{\\mathrm{bs}} = -K e (T_{e}+T_{i}) \\left( \\frac{(V_{\\mathrm{NC}} + V_{p})n}{D_{\\mathrm{NC}} + D_{\\mathrm{QL}}} - \\frac{V_{\\mathrm{NC}}\\,n}{D_{\\mathrm{NC}}} \\right)\n$$\nThis can be simplified by factoring out $n$ and combining the terms in the bracket:\n$$\n\\Delta j_{\\mathrm{bs}} = -K e n (T_{e}+T_{i}) \\left( \\frac{D_{\\mathrm{NC}}(V_{\\mathrm{NC}} + V_{p}) - V_{\\mathrm{NC}}(D_{\\mathrm{NC}} + D_{\\mathrm{QL}})}{D_{\\mathrm{NC}}(D_{\\mathrm{NC}} + D_{\\mathrm{QL}})} \\right)\n$$\n$$\n\\Delta j_{\\mathrm{bs}} = -K e n (T_{e}+T_{i}) \\left( \\frac{D_{\\mathrm{NC}}V_{p} - V_{\\mathrm{NC}}D_{\\mathrm{QL}}}{D_{\\mathrm{NC}}(D_{\\mathrm{NC}} + D_{\\mathrm{QL}})} \\right)\n$$\nNow we substitute the given numerical values:\n- $n = 6.0 \\times 10^{19}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-3}$\n- $T_{e} = 2.5\\,\\mathrm{keV} = 2.5 \\times 10^3\\,\\mathrm{eV}$\n- $T_{i} = 3.0\\,\\mathrm{keV} = 3.0 \\times 10^3\\,\\mathrm{eV}$\n- $D_{\\mathrm{NC}} = 0.05\\,\\mathrm{m}^{2}\\,\\mathrm{s}^{-1}$\n- $V_{\\mathrm{NC}} = -0.010\\,\\mathrm{m}\\,\\mathrm{s}^{-1}$\n- $D_{\\mathrm{QL}} = 0.50\\,\\mathrm{m}^{2}\\,\\mathrm{s}^{-1}$\n- $V_{p} = -0.050\\,\\mathrm{m}\\,\\mathrm{s}^{-1}$\n- $e = 1.602176634 \\times 10^{-19}\\,\\mathrm{C}$\n- $K = 0.35\\,\\mathrm{A}\\,(\\mathrm{Pa}\\,\\mathrm{m})^{-1}$\n\nFirst, we compute the term in the parentheses:\nThe numerator is $D_{\\mathrm{NC}}V_{p} - V_{\\mathrm{NC}}D_{\\mathrm{QL}}$:\n$$\n(0.05\\,\\mathrm{m}^{2}\\,\\mathrm{s}^{-1})(-0.050\\,\\mathrm{m}\\,\\mathrm{s}^{-1}) - (-0.010\\,\\mathrm{m}\\,\\mathrm{s}^{-1})(0.50\\,\\mathrm{m}^{2}\\,\\mathrm{s}^{-1}) = -0.0025 + 0.0050 = 0.0025\\,\\mathrm{m}^{3}\\,\\mathrm{s}^{-2}\n$$\nThe denominator is $D_{\\mathrm{NC}}(D_{\\mathrm{NC}} + D_{\\mathrm{QL}})$:\n$$\n(0.05\\,\\mathrm{m}^{2}\\,\\mathrm{s}^{-1})(0.05 + 0.50\\,\\mathrm{m}^{2}\\,\\mathrm{s}^{-1}) = (0.05)(0.55) = 0.0275\\,\\mathrm{m}^{4}\\,\\mathrm{s}^{-2}\n$$\nThe ratio is:\n$$\n\\frac{0.0025}{0.0275}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-1} = \\frac{1}{11}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-1}\n$$\nNext, we compute the prefactor $-K e n (T_{e}+T_{i})$. The term $e(T_{e}+T_{i})$ is the total thermal energy per particle pair in Joules.\n$$\nT_{e} + T_{i} = (2.5 + 3.0)\\,\\mathrm{keV} = 5.5 \\times 10^{3}\\,\\mathrm{eV}\n$$\n$$\ne(T_{e}+T_{i}) = (1.602176634 \\times 10^{-19}\\,\\mathrm{J/eV}) \\times (5.5 \\times 10^{3}\\,\\mathrm{eV}) \\approx 8.81197 \\times 10^{-16}\\,\\mathrm{J}\n$$\nThe total thermal pressure contribution is $p_{th} = n\\,e\\,(T_{e}+T_{i})$:\n$$\n(6.0 \\times 10^{19}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-3})(8.81197 \\times 10^{-16}\\,\\mathrm{J}) \\approx 52871.8\\,\\mathrm{J}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-3} = 52871.8\\,\\mathrm{Pa}\n$$\nNow we assemble $\\Delta j_{\\mathrm{bs}}$:\n$$\n\\Delta j_{\\mathrm{bs}} = -K \\left( n\\,e\\,(T_{e}+T_{i}) \\right) \\left( \\frac{D_{\\mathrm{NC}}V_{p} - V_{\\mathrm{NC}}D_{\\mathrm{QL}}}{D_{\\mathrm{NC}}(D_{\\mathrm{NC}} + D_{\\mathrm{QL}})} \\right)\n$$\n$$\n\\Delta j_{\\mathrm{bs}} = -(0.35\\,\\mathrm{A}\\,(\\mathrm{Pa}\\,\\mathrm{m})^{-1}) \\times (52871.8\\,\\mathrm{Pa}) \\times \\left(\\frac{1}{11}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-1}\\right)\n$$\n$$\n\\Delta j_{\\mathrm{bs}} \\approx -(18505.13\\,\\mathrm{A}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-1}) \\times \\left(\\frac{1}{11}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-1}\\right)\n$$\n$$\n\\Delta j_{\\mathrm{bs}} \\approx -1682.285\\,\\mathrm{A}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-2}\n$$\nThe physical interpretation confirms this result. The enhanced outward transport due to turbulence flattens the density profile. This reduces the magnitude of the total pressure gradient, which in turn reduces the magnitude of the bootstrap current. Thus, the change $\\Delta j_{\\mathrm{bs}} = j_{\\mathrm{bs}}^{\\mathrm{NC+QL}} - j_{\\mathrm{bs}}^{\\mathrm{NC}}$ must be negative.\n\nRounding the result to four significant figures gives $-1682\\,\\mathrm{A}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-2}$. Expressed in standard scientific notation, this is $-1.682 \\times 10^{3}\\,\\mathrm{A}\\,\\mathrm{m}^{-2}$."}]


title: [multiple_choice] Analyze how $Z_{\mathrm{eff}}$ modifies the Spitzer resistivity profile and discuss implications for sawtooth period and current profile control in the presence of impurities.

Question: A large-aspect-ratio, circular tokamak of minor radius $a$ is operated in an ohmically assisted regime with additional heating. The electron temperature profile is approximately parabolic, $T_{e}(r)=T_{c}-\Delta T\,(r/a)^{2}$, with $T_{c}=3\,\mathrm{keV}$ and $\Delta T=2\,\mathrm{keV}$, so that $T_{e}(0)=3\,\mathrm{keV}$ and $T_{e}(a)=1\,\mathrm{keV}$. The safety factor $q=1$ surface is located at $r_{1}=0.3\,a$. The effective ion charge (quantifying the average impurity content), $Z_{\mathrm{eff}}(r)$, takes one of three forms depending on impurity transport: (i) uniform $Z_{\mathrm{eff}}(r)=Z_{0}$, with $Z_{0}=2$; (ii) edge-peaked (screened core), $Z_{\mathrm{eff}}(r)=1.5+1.5\,(r/a)^{2}$; (iii) core-peaked (accumulation), $Z_{\mathrm{eff}}(r)=1.5+1.5\,[1-(r/a)^{2}]$. Assume a typical Coulomb logarithm that is radially weakly varying and may be treated as constant over the small region around $r_{1}$, and that the electron density profile is sufficiently flat near $r_{1}$ to neglect its radial variation for scaling arguments.

Starting from the relation between electrical conductivity and electron collision frequency, and from Maxwell–Faraday and Ampère’s laws, analyze how $Z_{\mathrm{eff}}(r)$ modifies the Spitzer resistivity profile $\eta(r)$ and how this impacts the local resistive diffusion time near $r_{1}$. Use these results to infer qualitative implications for the sawtooth period scaling and for current profile control (e.g., maintenance of localized non-inductive current) in the presence of impurities, including the roles of impurity screening versus core accumulation and the possibility of radiation-driven temperature reduction.

Which of the following statements are consistent with first-principles scaling and the provided profiles?

A. For a fixed $T_{e}(r)$, a uniform increase in $Z_{\mathrm{eff}}$ increases the Spitzer resistivity $\eta(r)$ everywhere and reduces the local resistive diffusion time near $r_{1}$, tending to shorten the sawtooth period.

B. With impurity screening that produces an edge-peaked $Z_{\mathrm{eff}}(r)$, the resistivity at $r_{1}$ is reduced relative to the uniform $Z_{\mathrm{eff}}$ case, leading to a longer sawtooth period; simultaneously, the increased edge resistivity shifts inductive current density toward the core, making the total current density profile more peaked.

C. Core impurity accumulation (core-peaked $Z_{\mathrm{eff}}(r)$) reduces current diffusion locally, delaying the sawtooth crash and aiding steady non-inductive current profile control near $r_{1}$.

D. Increasing $Z_{\mathrm{eff}}$ increases the electron–ion collision frequency, which both reduces non-inductive current-drive efficiency and increases the diffusion of any driven current, making current profile control more difficult; in extreme impurity accumulation, enhanced radiation can depress $T_{e}(r)$, steeply increasing $\eta(r)$ and potentially precipitating a radiation collapse prior to effective sawtooth control.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{ABD}$$", "solution": "The problem statement has been critically evaluated and is determined to be valid. It is scientifically grounded in established principles of plasma physics, well-posed for a qualitative analysis, and objectively formulated. All provided data and profiles are physically plausible for a tokamak plasma. We may therefore proceed with the solution.\n\nThe analysis hinges on the interplay between the effective ion charge $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r)$, the electron temperature $T_e(r)$, the Spitzer resistivity $\\eta(r)$, the resistive diffusion time $\\tau_R$, and their consequences for magnetohydrodynamic (MHD) stability (specifically, sawteeth) and current profile control.\n\nFirst, we establish the fundamental physical relations.\n1.  **Spitzer Resistivity ($\\eta$)**: For a magnetized plasma, the electrical resistivity parallel to the magnetic field is given by the Spitzer-Härm formula. Its dependence on local plasma parameters is approximately\n    $$ \\eta(r) \\propto \\frac{Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r) \\ln\\Lambda}{T_e(r)^{3/2}} $$\n    where $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ is the effective ion charge, $T_e$ is the electron temperature, and $\\ln\\Lambda$ is the Coulomb logarithm. The problem states we can treat $\\ln\\Lambda$ as constant. Thus, the key scaling is:\n    $$ \\eta(r) \\propto \\frac{Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r)}{T_e(r)^{3/2}} $$\n\n2.  **Resistive Diffusion of Magnetic Field**: The evolution of the magnetic field $\\mathbf{B}$ in a resistive plasma is governed by combining Maxwell-Faraday's law ($\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{E} = -\\partial \\mathbf{B} / \\partial t$) and Ampère's law ($\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\mu_0 \\mathbf{J}$) with Ohm's law ($\\mathbf{E} + \\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\eta \\mathbf{J}$). In the rest frame of the plasma, this simplifies to $\\mathbf{E} = \\eta \\mathbf{J}$. Combining these yields the magnetic diffusion equation:\n    $$ \\frac{\\partial \\mathbf{B}}{\\partial t} = \\frac{1}{\\mu_0} \\nabla \\times (\\eta \\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B}) $$\n    This equation shows that magnetic structures diffuse over a characteristic time scale $\\tau_R$, which for a length scale $L$ scales as:\n    $$ \\tau_R \\sim \\frac{\\mu_0 L^2}{\\eta} $$\n    A higher resistivity $\\eta$ leads to a shorter diffusion time, meaning magnetic fields and associated currents diffuse more rapidly.\n\n3.  **Sawtooth Oscillations**: Sawtooth crashes are triggered by the growth of an $m=1, n=1$ internal resistive kink mode at the safety factor $q=1$ surface, located at radius $r_1$. The period of the sawteeth, $\\tau_{ST}$, is related to the growth time of this instability. In many models, a slower resistive reconnection at the $q=1$ surface leads to a longer sawtooth period. The characteristic time for this resistive process is the local resistive diffusion time, $\\tau_R(r_1)$. Therefore, a longer $\\tau_R(r_1)$ is associated with a longer sawtooth period. This gives the scaling:\n    $$ \\tau_{ST} \\propto \\tau_R(r_1) \\propto \\frac{1}{\\eta(r_1)} $$\n\nWith these principles, we evaluate the given $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r)$ profiles at the $q=1$ surface, $r_1 = 0.3\\,a$. The electron temperature at this location is fixed for the initial part of the analysis (as per options A, B, C): $T_e(r_1) = T_c - \\Delta T (r_1/a)^2 = 3\\,\\mathrm{keV} - 2\\,\\mathrm{keV} \\times (0.3)^2 = 3 - 0.18 = 2.82\\,\\mathrm{keV}$. Since $T_e(r_1)$ is fixed, the local resistivity $\\eta(r_1)$ is directly proportional to the local effective charge $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r_1)$.\n\nWe calculate $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r_1)$ for the three cases:\n(i) Uniform: $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r_1) = 2$.\n(ii) Edge-peaked: $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r_1) = 1.5 + 1.5\\,(0.3)^2 = 1.5 + 0.135 = 1.635$.\n(iii) Core-peaked: $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r_1) = 1.5 + 1.5\\,[1-(0.3)^2] = 1.5 + 1.5 \\times 0.91 = 1.5 + 1.365 = 2.865$.\n\nNow we analyze each option.\n\n**A. For a fixed $T_{e}(r)$, a uniform increase in $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ increases the Spitzer resistivity $\\eta(r)$ everywhere and reduces the local resistive diffusion time near $r_{1}$, tending to shorten the sawtooth period.**\n\n*   From $\\eta(r) \\propto Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r)/T_e(r)^{3/2}$, if $T_e(r)$ is fixed and $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ is increased uniformly, $\\eta(r)$ increases at every radial position $r$. This part is correct.\n*   The local resistive diffusion time is $\\tau_R(r_1) \\propto 1/\\eta(r_1)$. Since $\\eta(r_1)$ increases, $\\tau_R(r_1)$ must decrease. This part is correct.\n*   The sawtooth period scales as $\\tau_{ST} \\propto \\tau_R(r_1)$. A decrease in $\\tau_R(r_1)$ implies a shortening of the sawtooth period. This part is correct.\n*   Verdict: **Correct**.\n\n**B. With impurity screening that produces an edge-peaked $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r)$, the resistivity at $r_{1}$ is reduced relative to the uniform $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ case, leading to a longer sawtooth period; simultaneously, the increased edge resistivity shifts inductive current density toward the core, making the total current density profile more peaked.**\n\n*   We compare the edge-peaked case (ii) to the uniform case (i). At $r_1=0.3\\,a$, we calculated $Z_{\\mathrm{eff},(ii)}(r_1) = 1.635$ and $Z_{\\mathrm{eff},(i)}(r_1) = 2$. Since $Z_{\\mathrm{eff},(ii)}(r_1) < Z_{\\mathrm{eff},(i)}(r_1)$, the local resistivity $\\eta(r_1)$ is indeed reduced. This is correct.\n*   A reduced $\\eta(r_1)$ leads to a longer resistive time $\\tau_R(r_1)$ and thus a longer sawtooth period $\\tau_{ST}$. This part is correct.\n*   Now we consider the effect on the current profile. In steady-state ohmic conditions, the toroidal electric field is uniform, so the current density profile is $J_{OH}(r) \\propto 1/\\eta(r)$. The edge-peaked profile is $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r)=1.5+1.5\\,(r/a)^{2}$. Towards the edge ($r \\to a$), $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r)$ increases, making the edge resistivity higher than in regions closer to the core. This high edge resistivity suppresses the inductive current at the plasma edge, forcing it to flow preferentially in the lower-resistivity core. This leads to a more centrally peaked current density profile compared to a case with uniform or core-peaked resistivity. This statement is physically correct.\n*   Verdict: **Correct**.\n\n**C. Core impurity accumulation (core-peaked $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r)$) reduces current diffusion locally, delaying the sawtooth crash and aiding steady non-inductive current profile control near $r_{1}$.**\n\n*   Core impurity accumulation corresponds to case (iii), where $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r_1) = 2.865$. This is higher than in the uniform case ($2$).\n*   A higher $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r_1)$ leads to a higher local resistivity $\\eta(r_1)$.\n*   The statement claims this \"reduces current diffusion\". This is incorrect. The rate of magnetic diffusion is proportional to $\\eta$. A higher $\\eta$ leads to *faster* diffusion, not slower. The characteristic time for diffusion, $\\tau_R \\propto 1/\\eta$, becomes shorter.\n*   The statement claims this is \"delaying the sawtooth crash\". This is also incorrect. A higher $\\eta(r_1)$ means a shorter $\\tau_R(r_1)$, which is expected to *shorten* the sawtooth period, not delay it.\n*   Finally, the statement claims this is \"aiding steady non-inductive current profile control\". Faster diffusion means any locally driven current profile will broaden and dissipate more quickly, making it *harder* to maintain a specific profile shape. This is the opposite of aiding control.\n*   Verdict: **Incorrect**.\n\n**D. Increasing $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ increases the electron–ion collision frequency, which both reduces non-inductive current-drive efficiency and increases the diffusion of any driven current, making current profile control more difficult; in extreme impurity accumulation, enhanced radiation can depress $T_{e}(r)$, steeply increasing $\\eta(r)$ and potentially precipitating a radiation collapse prior to effective sawtooth control.**\n\n*   This statement presents a sequence of interconnected physical effects. We validate them step-by-step.\n*   The electron-ion collision frequency scales as $\\nu_{ei} \\propto n_e Z_{\\mathrm{eff}} / T_e^{3/2}$. Increasing $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ directly increases $\\nu_{ei}$. Correct.\n*   Non-inductive current drive (NICD) efficiency depends on creating and sustaining a population of current-carrying supra-thermal electrons. Higher collisionality ($\\nu_{ei}$) leads to faster pitch-angle scattering and momentum loss for these electrons, thus reducing the current drive efficiency. So, increased $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ reduces NICD efficiency. Correct.\n*   As established, an increase in $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ increases $\\eta$. Since current diffuses at a rate related to $\\eta$, this increases the diffusion of any driven current. Correct.\n*   The combination of lower efficiency and faster diffusion makes it more difficult and power-intensive to maintain a desired non-inductive current profile. Correct.\n*   Impurities, especially if not fully stripped, are potent radiators. \"Extreme impurity accumulation\" implies a high density of impurity ions, which leads to enhanced line and bremsstrahlung radiation. This radiation is a power loss channel that can locally cool the plasma, depressing $T_e(r)$. Correct.\n*   Resistivity is $\\eta \\propto Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}/T_e^{3/2}$. If impurity accumulation both increases $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ and decreases $T_e$, both effects serve to increase $\\eta$. The dependence on $T_e$ is particularly strong ($T_e^{-3/2}$), so a modest drop in temperature can cause a steep rise in resistivity. Correct.\n*   A \"radiation collapse\" is a thermal instability where radiative losses exceed local heating, leading to a feedback loop of cooling, increased resistivity, and further cooling, which can terminate the discharge. This is a real and dangerous phenomenon in tokamaks, often triggered by excessive impurity accumulation. The statement that this can precipitate is correct. The possibility of it happening before a desired sawtooth control scheme can be established is a plausible operational scenario.\n*   Verdict: **Correct**.\n\nIn summary, statements A, B, and D are consistent with the provided information and established principles of plasma physics. Statement C contains multiple incorrect assertions."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{ABD}$$", "solution": "We proceed from first principles. The electrical conductivity $\\sigma$ of a fully ionized plasma dominated by electron–ion Coulomb collisions satisfies\n$$\n\\sigma=\\frac{n_{e} e^{2}}{m_{e}\\,\\nu_{e}},\n$$\nwhere $n_{e}$ is the electron density, $e$ is the elementary charge, $m_{e}$ is the electron mass, and $\\nu_{e}$ is the effective electron collision frequency with charges in the plasma. For Coulomb collisions in a multispecies plasma, the electron–ion collision frequency scales as\n$$\n\\nu_{e i}\\propto Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}\\, n_{e}\\,\\ln\\Lambda\\, T_{e}^{-3/2},\n$$\nwhere $\\ln\\Lambda$ is the Coulomb logarithm and $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}=\\sum_{j} n_{j} Z_{j}^{2}/\\sum_{j} n_{j} Z_{j}$ weights by charge state $Z_{j}$ and density $n_{j}$. For the regime in question (near $r_{1}$), we treat $\\ln\\Lambda$ and $n_{e}$ as approximately constant in $r$. The Spitzer resistivity $\\eta$ is the inverse of $\\sigma$,\n$$\n\\eta=\\sigma^{-1}=\\frac{m_{e}\\,\\nu_{e}}{n_{e} e^{2}},\n$$\nso, with electron–ion collisions dominant, the radial scaling is\n$$\n\\eta(r)\\propto \\frac{Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r)\\,\\ln\\Lambda}{T_{e}(r)^{3/2}}.\n$$\nThus, higher $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ increases $\\eta$, while higher $T_{e}$ decreases $\\eta$ as $T_{e}^{-3/2}$.\n\nTo relate $\\eta$ to resistive diffusion, combine Maxwell–Faraday, $\\partial \\mathbf{B}/\\partial t=-\\nabla\\times \\mathbf{E}$, with Ohm’s law, $\\mathbf{E}=\\eta\\,\\mathbf{j}$, and Ampère’s law (magnetostatic form), $\\nabla\\times \\mathbf{B}=\\mu_{0}\\mathbf{j}$. Eliminating $\\mathbf{E}$ and $\\mathbf{j}$ yields the magnetic diffusion equation\n$$\n\\frac{\\partial \\mathbf{B}}{\\partial t}=\\frac{\\eta}{\\mu_{0}}\\,\\nabla^{2}\\mathbf{B},\n$$\nunder the assumption that $\\eta$ varies weakly on the scale of interest. The corresponding local magnetic (current) diffusion time associated with a characteristic length $L$ is\n$$\n\\tau_{R}\\sim \\frac{\\mu_{0}\\,L^{2}}{\\eta}.\n$$\nNear the $q=1$ surface, taking $L$ comparable to $r_{1}$ or a local shear length, we see that a larger $\\eta$ implies a shorter $\\tau_{R}$, and since many sawtooth models scale the sawtooth period $\\tau_{s}$ with the local resistive time at $q=1$ (up to model-dependent factors), a larger $\\eta$ tends to shorten $\\tau_{s}$, all else equal.\n\nWe now evaluate the effect of the three $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ profiles at $r_{1}=0.3\\,a$. The prescribed electron temperature profile is $T_{e}(r)=T_{c}-\\Delta T\\,(r/a)^{2}$ with $T_{c}=3\\,\\mathrm{keV}$ and $\\Delta T=2\\,\\mathrm{keV}$. At $r_{1}$, $(r_{1}/a)^{2}=(0.3)^{2}=0.09$, so\n$$\nT_{e}(r_{1})=3\\,\\mathrm{keV}-2\\,\\mathrm{keV}\\times 0.09=2.82\\,\\mathrm{keV},\n$$\nand\n$$\nT_{e}(r_{1})^{3/2}\\approx (2.82)^{3/2}\\approx 2.82\\times \\sqrt{2.82}\\approx 2.82\\times 1.679\\approx 4.737\\ \\text{(in arbitrary units)}.\n$$\nFor the three $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ cases:\n- Uniform: $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r_{1})=2.0$.\n- Edge-peaked (screening): $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r_{1})=1.5+1.5\\times 0.09=1.635$.\n- Core-peaked (accumulation): $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r_{1})=1.5+1.5\\times(1-0.09)=1.5+1.365=2.865$.\n\nUsing $\\eta(r)\\propto Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r)/T_{e}(r)^{3/2}$ with the same $T_{e}(r_{1})$ across cases, we obtain relative resistivities at $r_{1}$:\n$$\n\\eta_{\\text{uniform}}(r_{1})\\propto \\frac{2.000}{4.737}\\approx 0.423,\\quad\n\\eta_{\\text{screened}}(r_{1})\\propto \\frac{1.635}{4.737}\\approx 0.345,\\quad\n\\eta_{\\text{accum}}(r_{1})\\propto \\frac{2.865}{4.737}\\approx 0.605.\n$$\nTherefore, compared to the uniform case, impurity screening (edge-peaked $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$) reduces $\\eta(r_{1})$ and increases the local resistive time $\\tau_{R}(r_{1})$, while core accumulation increases $\\eta(r_{1})$ and decreases $\\tau_{R}(r_{1})$. Under models where $\\tau_{s}$ scales with $\\tau_{R}(r_{1})$, screening tends to lengthen the sawtooth period; accumulation tends to shorten it.\n\nWe also consider current profile control. Non-inductive current-drive efficiency for radio-frequency and beam-driven methods typically degrades with increasing effective collisionality, which increases with $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ and decreasing $T_{e}$. Additionally, the diffusion of driven current is governed by the same magnetic diffusion coefficient $D\\sim \\eta/\\mu_{0}$, so larger $\\eta$ accelerates the radial spreading of localized current, complicating profile control. Regarding radiation, the volumetric bremsstrahlung loss scales as $P_{\\mathrm{br}}\\propto Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}\\,n_{e}^{2}\\,T_{e}^{1/2}$ in a fully ionized plasma, and line radiation from partially ionized impurities can add further losses, especially off-axis. Core impurity accumulation increases $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ and can raise $P_{\\mathrm{rad}}$ in the core; if $P_{\\mathrm{rad}}$ exceeds available heating such that $dW/dt<0$, $T_{e}$ drops, which increases $\\eta\\propto T_{e}^{-3/2}$, potentially creating a positive feedback toward a radiation collapse if not mitigated.\n\nWe now assess each option:\n\nA. This states that, for fixed $T_{e}(r)$, increasing uniform $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ increases $\\eta(r)$ everywhere and reduces the local resistive diffusion time near $r_{1}$, tending to shorten the sawtooth period. From $\\eta\\propto Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}/T_{e}^{3/2}$ and $\\tau_{R}\\sim \\mu_{0}L^{2}/\\eta$, the statement is consistent: larger uniform $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ implies larger $\\eta$ and shorter $\\tau_{R}$, hence a shorter $\\tau_{s}$ in models where $\\tau_{s}\\propto \\tau_{R}(r_{1})$. Verdict: Correct.\n\nB. This claims that edge-peaked $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}(r)$ (screening) reduces $\\eta$ at $r_{1}$ relative to the uniform case, lengthening the sawtooth period, and that increased edge resistivity shifts inductive current density toward the core, making the current density profile more peaked. Numerically, we found $\\eta_{\\text{screened}}(r_{1})<\\eta_{\\text{uniform}}(r_{1})$, implying longer $\\tau_{R}(r_{1})$ and thus longer $\\tau_{s}$ under common scaling. For inductive current with a given toroidal electric field $E_{\\phi}(r)$, $j_{\\phi}(r)=\\sigma(r) E_{\\phi}(r)\\propto E_{\\phi}(r)/\\eta(r)$; if $\\eta$ is larger at the edge (edge-peaked $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$), then $j_{\\phi}$ is reduced at the edge for similar $E_{\\phi}$, yielding a more peaked $j_{\\phi}(r)$. Verdict: Correct.\n\nC. This asserts that core impurity accumulation reduces current diffusion, delays the sawtooth crash, and aids non-inductive current profile control near $r_{1}$. In fact, core accumulation increases $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ near $r_{1}$, increasing $\\eta$ and thus increasing the diffusion coefficient $D\\sim \\eta/\\mu_{0}$, which accelerates current diffusion rather than reducing it. Moreover, larger $\\eta$ at $r_{1}$ reduces $\\tau_{R}(r_{1})$ and tends to shorten (not delay) the sawtooth period under typical scaling. Higher $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ also reduces current-drive efficiency and makes maintaining a localized driven current more difficult. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nD. This states that increasing $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ raises the electron–ion collision frequency, which reduces non-inductive current-drive efficiency and increases the diffusion of driven current, complicating profile control; further, strong impurity accumulation can enhance radiation, depress $T_{e}$, steeply increase $\\eta$, and potentially trigger radiation collapse before effective sawtooth control. From $\\nu_{e i}\\propto Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}\\,n_{e}\\,\\ln\\Lambda\\,T_{e}^{-3/2}$ and $\\eta\\propto Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}/T_{e}^{3/2}$, larger $Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ increases collisionality and resistivity, reducing current-drive efficiency and increasing diffusion of driven current. Radiation losses ($P_{\\mathrm{br}}\\propto Z_{\\mathrm{eff}}\\,n_{e}^{2}\\,T_{e}^{1/2}$ plus line radiation) can indeed lower $T_{e}$, which further increases $\\eta$ and can lead to a positive feedback culminating in radiation collapse if heating is insufficient. Verdict: Correct.\n\nTherefore, options A, B, and D are consistent with the first-principles analysis and the provided profiles, while option C is not."}]</wiki_content6.1>

<wiki_content6.2>title: [multiple_choice] Discuss how anomalous transport models should not be treated as sources and sinks and rigorously differentiate between transport coefficients and reaction terms.
