title: [multiple_choice] Define the H-mode power threshold scaling and discuss its implications for access to H-mode in a burning plasma with significant alpha heating.

Question: A High-confinement mode (H-mode) transition in a tokamak is observed when turbulent transport at the plasma edge is suppressed and a transport barrier forms near the separatrix. Empirically, the minimum net power flowing across the separatrix required to trigger this transition is known as the H-mode power threshold. In an advanced burning plasma, alpha-particle heating from deuterium-tritium fusion contributes to the core power balance and can modify the net power flowing to the edge.

Starting from global energy conservation for the confined plasma,
$$\frac{dW}{dt} = P_{\mathrm{aux}} + P_{\alpha} - P_{\mathrm{rad}} - P_{\mathrm{sep}},$$
where $W$ is the stored thermal energy, $P_{\mathrm{aux}}$ is externally applied heating, $P_{\alpha}$ is alpha-particle heating, $P_{\mathrm{rad}}$ is core radiation inside the separatrix, and $P_{\mathrm{sep}}$ is the net power flowing across the separatrix into the scrape-off layer, the H-mode power threshold condition can be expressed conceptually as
$$P_{\mathrm{sep}} \ge P_{\mathrm{th}}(\text{operating point}),$$
where $P_{\mathrm{th}}$ is an empirically scaled threshold that depends on plasma parameters such as edge density $n_e$, toroidal magnetic field $B_T$, size metrics (e.g., plasma surface area), and isotope mass.

Consider an ITER-like scenario with edge density $n_e \approx 8\times 10^{19}\ \mathrm{m}^{-3}$, toroidal magnetic field $B_T \approx 5.3\ \mathrm{T}$, and large device size. Assume a near-steady pre-H-mode phase with $P_{\mathrm{aux}} = 50\ \mathrm{MW}$, $P_{\alpha} = 0\ \mathrm{MW}$, $P_{\mathrm{rad}} = 30\ \mathrm{MW}$, and $\frac{dW}{dt} \approx 0$, followed by a burning phase with $P_{\mathrm{aux}} = 50\ \mathrm{MW}$, $P_{\alpha} = 100\ \mathrm{MW}$, $P_{\mathrm{rad}} = 30\ \mathrm{MW}$, and $\frac{dW}{dt} \approx 0$. Using the global energy balance, reason about the net power crossing the separatrix in both phases and how the empirical scaling of $P_{\mathrm{th}}$ influences access to H-mode in the burning phase.

Which option best defines the H-mode power threshold scaling and correctly states its implications for access to H-mode in a burning plasma with significant alpha heating?

A. The H-mode threshold is defined in terms of the net power crossing the separatrix, $P_{\mathrm{sep}}$, which must exceed an empirically scaled $P_{\mathrm{th}}(n_e, B_T, \text{size}, \text{isotope mass})$ that increases with $n_e$ and $B_T$ and roughly with machine size. In a burning plasma, $P_{\alpha}$ adds directly to $P_{\mathrm{sep}}$, reducing the required $P_{\mathrm{aux}}$ to reach and sustain H-mode; alpha heating does not itself reduce $P_{\mathrm{th}}$ but helps satisfy $P_{\mathrm{sep}} \ge P_{\mathrm{th}}$.

B. The H-mode threshold is the sum $P_{\mathrm{aux}} + P_{\alpha}$ and is independent of $n_e$, $B_T$, and machine size. Alpha heating increases the threshold, so more $P_{\mathrm{aux}}$ is needed to access H-mode in a burning plasma.

C. The H-mode threshold is determined solely by the core stored energy $W$; when $\frac{dW}{dt} = 0$, H-mode cannot be achieved. In burning plasmas, $P_{\alpha}$ does not influence access because it is deposited in the core rather than at the edge.

D. The H-mode threshold is defined by the power to the divertor plates and decreases with machine size. Alpha heating reduces $P_{\mathrm{sep}}$ by improving core confinement, making H-mode access more difficult in burning plasmas.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "Begin with energy conservation for the confined plasma region,\n$$\\frac{dW}{dt} = P_{\\mathrm{aux}} + P_{\\alpha} - P_{\\mathrm{rad}} - P_{\\mathrm{sep}}.$$\nThis identity follows from the First Law of Thermodynamics applied to the plasma volume bounded by the separatrix, where the net inflow of power equals the rate of change of stored energy plus the outflow across the boundary and volumetric radiation inside the boundary.\n\nSolving for the power across the separatrix,\n$$P_{\\mathrm{sep}} = P_{\\mathrm{aux}} + P_{\\alpha} - P_{\\mathrm{rad}} - \\frac{dW}{dt}.$$\nThe H-mode power threshold is an empirical requirement on $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$:\n$$P_{\\mathrm{sep}} \\ge P_{\\mathrm{th}}(\\text{operating point}),$$\nwhere $P_{\\mathrm{th}}$ depends on parameters that affect edge turbulence suppression and shear layer formation. Broad experimental evidence indicates that $P_{\\mathrm{th}}$ increases with edge density $n_e$, increases with toroidal magnetic field $B_T$, and grows with device size metrics (e.g., plasma surface area or major radius), with a reduction for heavier hydrogen isotopes (isotope effect). The quantity controlled in experiments and relevant to the threshold is $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$, not simply the external power $P_{\\mathrm{aux}}$.\n\nCompute $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$ in the pre-H-mode phase (near steady state with $\\frac{dW}{dt}\\approx 0$):\n$$P_{\\mathrm{sep}}^{\\text{pre}} = 50\\ \\mathrm{MW} + 0\\ \\mathrm{MW} - 30\\ \\mathrm{MW} - 0 \\approx 20\\ \\mathrm{MW}.$$\nIn the burning phase (still near steady state),\n$$P_{\\mathrm{sep}}^{\\text{burn}} = 50\\ \\mathrm{MW} + 100\\ \\mathrm{MW} - 30\\ \\mathrm{MW} - 0 \\approx 120\\ \\mathrm{MW}.$$\nThus, the inclusion of alpha heating increases $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$ by approximately $100\\ \\mathrm{MW}$, meaning that for the same $P_{\\mathrm{aux}}$, the net edge power is much larger in the burning phase. Because $P_{\\mathrm{th}}$ is a property of the operating point and does not directly change due to the presence of alpha heating, alpha heating’s role is to help satisfy the inequality $P_{\\mathrm{sep}} \\ge P_{\\mathrm{th}}$. Therefore, in a burning plasma where $P_{\\alpha}$ is significant, the required external heating $P_{\\mathrm{aux}}$ to reach and sustain H-mode is reduced compared to a non-burning scenario, provided other parameters ($n_e$, $B_T$, size, impurity content) do not raise $P_{\\mathrm{th}}$ beyond reach. Additionally, at high $n_e$ and $B_T$ in large devices, $P_{\\mathrm{th}}$ can be substantial, so auxiliary heating may still be necessary to initiate the H-mode before $P_{\\alpha}$ becomes large.\n\nOption-by-option analysis:\n\nA. This option correctly identifies that the threshold is defined in terms of the net power crossing the separatrix, $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$, and that the threshold itself is an empirical scaling in terms of $n_e$, $B_T$, machine size, and isotope mass. It correctly states that $P_{\\alpha}$ increases $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$ and thus lowers the required $P_{\\mathrm{aux}}$ to meet the threshold, without asserting that $P_{\\alpha}$ reduces $P_{\\mathrm{th}}$ (it does not; $P_{\\mathrm{th}}$ is a property of the operating conditions). This is consistent with the energy balance calculations showing that $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}^{\\text{burn}} \\approx 120\\ \\mathrm{MW}$ versus $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}^{\\text{pre}} \\approx 20\\ \\mathrm{MW}$. Verdict: Correct.\n\nB. This option incorrectly defines the threshold as $P_{\\mathrm{aux}} + P_{\\alpha}$ rather than the net $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$, ignoring radiation and the stored energy rate. It also incorrectly claims independence of $n_e$, $B_T$, and size, contrary to extensive empirical evidence. Furthermore, it states alpha heating increases the threshold, which is false; $P_{\\alpha}$ increases $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$ but does not increase $P_{\\mathrm{th}}$. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nC. This option incorrectly asserts that the threshold is determined solely by $W$ and that $\\frac{dW}{dt}=0$ prevents H-mode. In reality, H-mode access depends on $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$ exceeding $P_{\\mathrm{th}}$; $\\frac{dW}{dt}=0$ is compatible with both L-mode and H-mode steady states. It also incorrectly claims that $P_{\\alpha}$ does not influence access because it is deposited in the core; while $P_{\\alpha}$ is deposited in the core, it contributes to $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$ via global power balance unless fully balanced by increased $P_{\\mathrm{rad}}$ or other sinks. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nD. This option misidentifies the threshold as the power to divertor plates and claims it decreases with machine size, contrary to observations that the threshold tends to grow with size metrics. It also incorrectly states that alpha heating reduces $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$ by improving core confinement; by energy conservation, for fixed $P_{\\mathrm{aux}}$ and $P_{\\mathrm{rad}}$, adding $P_{\\alpha}$ increases $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$ at steady state. Verdict: Incorrect."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "## Problem Validation\n\n### Step 1: Extract Givens\nThe problem provides the following information:\n1.  **Global Energy Conservation Equation**:\n    $$\\frac{dW}{dt} = P_{\\mathrm{aux}} + P_{\\alpha} - P_{\\mathrm{rad}} - P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$$\n2.  **Variable Definitions**:\n    -   $W$: Stored thermal energy.\n    -   $P_{\\mathrm{aux}}$: Externally applied heating.\n    -   $P_{\\alpha}$: Alpha-particle heating.\n    -   $P_{\\mathrm{rad}}$: Core radiation inside the separatrix.\n    -   $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$: Net power flowing across the separatrix.\n3.  **H-mode Power Threshold Condition**:\n    $$P_{\\mathrm{sep}} \\ge P_{\\mathrm{th}}(\\text{operating point})$$\n4.  **Dependencies of the Threshold Power $P_{\\mathrm{th}}$**:\n    -   $P_{\\mathrm{th}}$ depends on edge density $n_e$, toroidal magnetic field $B_T$, size metrics, and isotope mass.\n5.  **Scenario Parameters**:\n    -   Edge density: $n_e \\approx 8\\times 10^{19}\\ \\mathrm{m}^{-3}$.\n    -   Toroidal magnetic field: $B_T \\approx 5.3\\ \\mathrm{T}$.\n6.  **Phase 1 (pre-H-mode)**:\n    -   $P_{\\mathrm{aux}} = 50\\ \\mathrm{MW}$.\n    -   $P_{\\alpha} = 0\\ \\mathrm{MW}$.\n    -   $P_{\\mathrm{rad}} = 30\\ \\mathrm{MW}$.\n    -   $\\frac{dW}{dt} \\approx 0$.\n7.  **Phase 2 (burning phase)**:\n    -   $P_{\\mathrm{aux}} = 50\\ \\mathrm{MW}$.\n    -   $P_{\\alpha} = 100\\ \\mathrm{MW}$.\n    -   $P_{\\mathrm{rad}} = 30\\ \\mathrm{MW}$.\n    -   $\\frac{dW}{dt} \\approx 0$.\n\n### Step 2: Validate Using Extracted Givens\n-   **Scientifically Grounded**: The problem is well-grounded in tokamak plasma physics. The global energy balance equation is a standard model. The concept of the L-H transition and an empirical power threshold, $P_{\\mathrm{th}}$, which depends on parameters like $n_e$, $B_T$, and machine size, is a central topic in experimental fusion research. The given values for plasma parameters and powers are realistic for a large-scale experiment like ITER.\n-   **Well-Posed**: The problem is well-posed. It provides a clear physical model (energy balance) and a condition ($P_{\\mathrm{sep}} \\ge P_{\\mathrm{th}}$) and asks for a conceptual interpretation of how alpha heating affects this condition. A unique and logical conclusion can be derived from the given information.\n-   **Objective**: The problem is stated in objective, technical language, free of bias or subjective claims. All terms are standard in the field of fusion science.\n-   **Completeness and Consistency**: The problem provides sufficient information to analyze the two scenarios and draw a conclusion. The assumption $\\frac{dW}{dt} \\approx 0$ is a standard and appropriate simplification for analyzing steady-state or quasi-steady-state power balance. There are no contradictions in the provided data.\n\n### Step 3: Verdict and Action\nThe problem statement is valid, scientifically sound, and well-posed. Proceeding to solution.\n\n## Solution Derivation\n\nThe analysis begins with the provided global energy conservation equation for the plasma volume inside the separatrix:\n$$\\frac{dW}{dt} = P_{\\mathrm{aux}} + P_{\\alpha} - P_{\\mathrm{rad}} - P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$$\nThe problem specifies that we are analyzing near-steady phases where the stored energy is approximately constant, so we take $\\frac{dW}{dt} \\approx 0$. This simplifies the power balance to:\n$$0 \\approx P_{\\mathrm{aux}} + P_{\\alpha} - P_{\\mathrm{rad}} - P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$$\nSolving for the net power crossing the separatrix, $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$, we find:\n$$P_{\\mathrm{sep}} \\approx P_{\\mathrm{aux}} + P_{\\alpha} - P_{\\mathrm{rad}}$$\nThis equation states that the power flowing out to the edge is the total heating power ($P_{\\mathrm{aux}} + P_{\\alpha}$) minus the power lost from the core via radiation ($P_{\\mathrm{rad}}$).\n\nThe condition for accessing H-mode is given as $P_{\\mathrm{sep}} \\ge P_{\\mathrm{th}}$. The threshold power, $P_{\\mathrm{th}}$, is an empirically determined function of plasma parameters, not a function of the heating mix.\n\nLet us apply the power balance equation to the two phases described:\n\n**Phase 1 (pre-H-mode, no alpha heating):**\n-   $P_{\\mathrm{aux}} = 50\\ \\mathrm{MW}$\n-   $P_{\\alpha} = 0\\ \\mathrm{MW}$\n-   $P_{\\mathrm{rad}} = 30\\ \\mathrm{MW}$\nThe net power crossing the separatrix is:\n$$P_{\\mathrm{sep},1} \\approx 50\\ \\mathrm{MW} + 0\\ \\mathrm{MW} - 30\\ \\mathrm{MW} = 20\\ \\mathrm{MW}$$\nSince this is a pre-H-mode phase, it implies that $P_{\\mathrm{sep},1} < P_{\\mathrm{th}}$ for the given operating conditions ($n_e$, $B_T$, etc.).\n\n**Phase 2 (burning phase, with alpha heating):**\n-   $P_{\\mathrm{aux}} = 50\\ \\mathrm{MW}$\n-   $P_{\\alpha} = 100\\ \\mathrm{MW}$\n-   $P_{\\mathrm{rad}} = 30\\ \\mathrm{MW}$\nThe net power crossing the separatrix is:\n$$P_{\\mathrm{sep},2} \\approx 50\\ \\mathrm{MW} + 100\\ \\mathrm{MW} - 30\\ \\mathrm{MW} = 120\\ \\mathrm{MW}$$\n\nBy comparing the two phases, we observe that $P_{\\mathrm{sep},2} \\gg P_{\\mathrm{sep},1}$. The alpha heating, $P_{\\alpha}$, acts as a significant additional source of power that contributes directly to the power flow across the separatrix, $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$. The threshold value $P_{\\mathrm{th}}$ depends on parameters such as $n_e$, $B_T$, and size, which are assumed to be a basis of the operating point, and is not directly a function of $P_{\\alpha}$ itself. Therefore, the effect of $P_{\\alpha}$ is to help satisfy the threshold condition $P_{\\mathrm{sep}} \\ge P_{\\mathrm{th}}$.\n\nThis implies that for a given required threshold $P_{\\mathrm{th}}$, the presence of significant alpha heating reduces the amount of external heating $P_{\\mathrm{aux}}$ needed to achieve H-mode. In this sense, alpha heating makes access to and sustainment of H-mode easier, as it provides a 'free' source of power flowing to the edge.\n\n## Option-by-Option Analysis\n\n**A. The H-mode threshold is defined in terms of the net power crossing the separatrix, $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$, which must exceed an empirically scaled $P_{\\mathrm{th}}(n_e, B_T, \\text{size}, \\text{isotope mass})$ that increases with $n_e$ and $B_T$ and roughly with machine size. In a burning plasma, $P_{\\alpha}$ adds directly to $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$, reducing the required $P_{\\mathrm{aux}}$ to reach and sustain H-mode; alpha heating does not itself reduce $P_{\\mathrm{th}}$ but helps satisfy $P_{\\mathrm{sep}} \\ge P_{\\mathrm{th}}$.**\nThis option correctly defines the threshold in terms of $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$ exceeding an empirically scaled $P_{\\mathrm{th}}$. It accurately reflects the known experimental dependencies of $P_{\\mathrm{th}}$ (increasing with density, field, and size). Crucially, it correctly interprets the role of $P_{\\alpha}$ from the power balance equation: $P_{\\alpha}$ is a source term that increases $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$ for a given $P_{\\mathrm{aux}}$ and $P_{\\mathrm{rad}}$, thus helping to meet the threshold condition and reducing the external power requirement. This statement is fully consistent with our derivation.\n**Verdict: Correct.**\n\n**B. The H-mode threshold is the sum $P_{\\mathrm{aux}} + P_{\\alpha}$ and is independent of $n_e$, $B_T$, and machine size. Alpha heating increases the threshold, so more $P_{\\mathrm{aux}}$ is needed to access H-mode in a burning plasma.**\nThis option is incorrect on multiple grounds. First, the relevant power is $P_{\\mathrm{sep}} = P_{\\mathrm{aux}} + P_{\\alpha} - P_{\\mathrm{rad}}$, not the total heating power $P_{\\mathrm{aux}} + P_{\\alpha}$. Core radiation is a significant sink. Second, it falsely claims the threshold is independent of plasma parameters, which contradicts both the problem statement and extensive experimental evidence. Third, it incorrectly concludes that alpha heating increases the threshold and makes H-mode access more difficult, which is the opposite of its effect.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**\n\n**C. The H-mode threshold is determined solely by the core stored energy $W$; when $\\frac{dW}{dt} = 0$, H-mode cannot be achieved. In burning plasmas, $P_{\\alpha}$ does not influence access because it is deposited in the core rather than at the edge.**\nThis option contains several fundamental errors. The H-mode threshold is a condition on power flow ($P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$), not stored energy ($W$). The condition $\\frac{dW}{dt} = 0$ signifies a steady-state in power, which is characteristic of a sustained H-mode, not a barrier to achieving it. The claim that $P_{\\alpha}$ does not influence access because it is deposited in the core is fallacious; energy deposited in the core must be transported outwards, and this transport of energy constitutes the power flow $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$ across the separatrix.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**\n\n**D. The H-mode threshold is defined by the power to the divertor plates and decreases with machine size. Alpha heating reduces $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$ by improving core confinement, making H-mode access more difficult in burning plasmas.**\nThis option is incorrect. The threshold is defined for power crossing the separatrix, $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$, not the power reaching the divertor plates (which is $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$ minus any radiation in the scrape-off layer and divertor region). It incorrectly claims the threshold decreases with size, whereas it is known to increase. It also falsely claims that alpha heating reduces $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$. From the power balance, $P_{\\alpha}$ is a heat source and thus increases $P_{\\mathrm{sep}}$. The claim that it makes H-mode access more difficult is the opposite of the correct conclusion.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**"}]


title: [calculation] L-H transition and pedestal formation

Question: Consider a large-aspect-ratio tokamak of major radius $R$ and minor radius $a$, with axisymmetric circular magnetic flux surfaces. Treat the plasma as a cylindrical annulus embedded in a torus so that the volume enclosed by a flux surface of minor radius $r$ is $V(r) = 2 \pi^{2} R r^{2}$ and the area of that surface is $S(r) = 4 \pi^{2} R r$. The plasma has uniform number density $n$ and receives uniform volumetric heating $s_{E}$, so that the total absorbed power is $P_{\text{abs}} = s_{E} V(a)$. Assume steady state and neglect convective energy losses, so that the net radial heat flow through a surface at $r$ equals the absorbed power within that surface, $Q(r) = s_{E} V(r)$. The corresponding radial heat flux density is $q(r) = Q(r)/S(r)$. Model cross-field energy transport as diffusive with an effective energy diffusivity $\chi(r)$ acting on the thermal energy density $w(r) = \frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T(r)$, where $k_{B}$ is the Boltzmann constant and $T(r)$ is the temperature. Under these assumptions the constitutive relation for radial heat flux is $q(r) = - \frac{3}{2} n k_{B} \chi(r) \frac{dT}{dr}$. Impose the boundary condition $T(a) = T_{\text{edge}}$.

Before the Low-to-High (L-H) transition, the plasma is in low-confinement regime with uniform energy diffusivity $\chi(r) = \chi_{L}$ throughout $0 \le r \le a$. After the L-H transition, a pedestal (edge transport barrier) of width $\Delta$ forms near the edge, with $\chi(r) = \chi_{\text{core}}$ for $0 \le r \le a - \Delta$ and $\chi(r) = \chi_{\text{ped}} \ll \chi_{\text{core}}$ for $a - \Delta < r \le a$. In both cases, take $T(a) = T_{\text{edge}}$ and $n$ and $s_{E}$ unchanged.

Using only the given geometric relations, steady-state energy conservation, and the diffusive constitutive law, derive the global energy content $W = \int w(r) \, dV$ and the energy confinement time $\tau_{E} = W/P_{\text{abs}}$ for both the pre-transition and post-transition states. Then compute the improvement factor $F \equiv \tau_{E}^{(\text{H})} / \tau_{E}^{(\text{L})}$.

Use the following parameter values:
- $R = 3.0 \,\text{m}$,
- $a = 1.0 \,\text{m}$,
- $n = 6.0 \times 10^{19} \,\text{m}^{-3}$,
- $T_{\text{edge}} = 150 \,\text{eV}$ (use $1 \,\text{eV} = 11604.51812 \,\text{K}$ to convert to Kelvin),
- $P_{\text{abs}} = 12.0 \times 10^{6} \,\text{W}$,
- $\chi_{L} = 1.0 \,\text{m}^{2} \,\text{s}^{-1}$,
- $\chi_{\text{core}} = 1.0 \,\text{m}^{2} \,\text{s}^{-1}$,
- $\chi_{\text{ped}} = 0.20 \,\text{m}^{2} \,\text{s}^{-1}$,
- $\Delta = 0.08 \,\text{m}$.

Round your final answer for $F$ to four significant figures and express it as a dimensionless number.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{2.045}$$", "solution": "The geometric relations for a large-aspect-ratio tokamak are taken as well-tested facts: the volume inside radius $r$ is $V(r) = 2 \\pi^{2} R r^{2}$ and the area of the surface at $r$ is $S(r) = 4 \\pi^{2} R r$. With uniform volumetric heating $s_{E}$, steady-state energy conservation gives\n$$\nQ(r) = s_{E} V(r) = s_{E} \\, 2 \\pi^{2} R r^{2},\n$$\nand hence the radial heat flux density is\n$$\nq(r) = \\frac{Q(r)}{S(r)} = \\frac{s_{E} \\, 2 \\pi^{2} R r^{2}}{4 \\pi^{2} R r} = \\frac{s_{E}}{2} \\, r.\n$$\nThe diffusive constitutive relation for heat flux acting on thermal energy density $w = \\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T$ is\n$$\nq(r) = - \\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} \\chi(r) \\frac{dT}{dr}.\n$$\nSolving for the temperature gradient,\n$$\n\\frac{dT}{dr} = - \\frac{q(r)}{\\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} \\chi(r)} = - \\frac{\\frac{s_{E}}{2} r}{\\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} \\chi(r)} = - \\frac{s_{E} \\, r}{3 n k_{B} \\chi(r)}.\n$$\nIntegrating from $r$ to the edge $a$ with boundary condition $T(a) = T_{\\text{edge}}$, the temperature profile is\n$$\nT(r) = T_{\\text{edge}} + \\int_{r}^{a} \\frac{s_{E} \\, \\rho}{3 n k_{B} \\chi(\\rho)} \\, d\\rho.\n$$\nThe global energy content is the integral of $w$ over volume:\n$$\nW = \\int_{0}^{a} \\left( \\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T(r) \\right) S(r) \\, dr = \\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} \\int_{0}^{a} T(r) \\, 4 \\pi^{2} R r \\, dr.\n$$\nSubstitute $T(r)$ and rearrange:\n$$\nW = \\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T_{\\text{edge}} \\int_{0}^{a} 4 \\pi^{2} R r \\, dr + \\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} \\int_{0}^{a} 4 \\pi^{2} R r \\left( \\int_{r}^{a} \\frac{s_{E} \\, \\rho}{3 n k_{B} \\chi(\\rho)} \\, d\\rho \\right) dr.\n$$\nThe first term evaluates to\n$$\n\\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T_{\\text{edge}} \\, 2 \\pi^{2} R a^{2} = \\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T_{\\text{edge}} \\, V(a).\n$$\nFor the second term, the factors $\\frac{3}{2} n k_{B}$ cancel, leaving\n$$\n\\int_{0}^{a} 4 \\pi^{2} R r \\left( \\int_{r}^{a} \\frac{s_{E} \\, \\rho}{\\chi(\\rho)} \\cdot \\frac{1}{3} \\, d\\rho \\right) dr = \\frac{4 \\pi^{2} R s_{E}}{3} \\int_{0}^{a} r \\left( \\int_{r}^{a} \\frac{\\rho}{\\chi(\\rho)} \\, d\\rho \\right) dr.\n$$\nA more direct route uses the previously simplified form when substituting $q(r)$:\n$$\nW = \\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T_{\\text{edge}} \\, V(a) + \\int_{0}^{a} 4 \\pi^{2} R r \\left( \\int_{r}^{a} \\frac{q(\\rho)}{\\chi(\\rho)} \\, d\\rho \\right) dr.\n$$\nSwap the order of integration in the second term. For any fixed $\\rho$, the inner integral over $r$ runs from $0$ to $\\rho$, yielding\n$$\nW = \\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T_{\\text{edge}} \\, V(a) + \\int_{0}^{a} \\left( \\int_{0}^{\\rho} 4 \\pi^{2} R r \\, dr \\right) \\frac{q(\\rho)}{\\chi(\\rho)} \\, d\\rho = \\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T_{\\text{edge}} \\, V(a) + \\int_{0}^{a} \\left( 2 \\pi^{2} R \\rho^{2} \\right) \\frac{q(\\rho)}{\\chi(\\rho)} \\, d\\rho.\n$$\nUsing $q(\\rho) = \\frac{s_{E}}{2} \\rho$, this becomes\n$$\nW = \\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T_{\\text{edge}} \\, V(a) + \\pi^{2} R s_{E} \\int_{0}^{a} \\frac{\\rho^{3}}{\\chi(\\rho)} \\, d\\rho.\n$$\nTherefore, the energy confinement time is\n$$\n\\tau_{E} = \\frac{W}{P_{\\text{abs}}} = \\frac{\\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T_{\\text{edge}} \\, V(a) + \\pi^{2} R s_{E} \\int_{0}^{a} \\frac{\\rho^{3}}{\\chi(\\rho)} \\, d\\rho}{s_{E} V(a)} = \\frac{\\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T_{\\text{edge}}}{s_{E}} + \\frac{\\pi^{2} R}{V(a)} \\int_{0}^{a} \\frac{\\rho^{3}}{\\chi(\\rho)} \\, d\\rho.\n$$\nUsing $V(a) = 2 \\pi^{2} R a^{2}$,\n$$\n\\tau_{E} = \\frac{\\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T_{\\text{edge}}}{s_{E}} + \\frac{1}{2 a^{2}} \\int_{0}^{a} \\frac{\\rho^{3}}{\\chi(\\rho)} \\, d\\rho.\n$$\n\nPre-transition (low-confinement) case with $\\chi(\\rho) = \\chi_{L}$:\n$$\n\\int_{0}^{a} \\frac{\\rho^{3}}{\\chi_{L}} \\, d\\rho = \\frac{1}{\\chi_{L}} \\cdot \\frac{a^{4}}{4} \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad \\tau_{E}^{(\\text{L})} = \\frac{\\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T_{\\text{edge}}}{s_{E}} + \\frac{1}{2 a^{2}} \\cdot \\frac{a^{4}}{4 \\chi_{L}} = \\frac{\\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T_{\\text{edge}}}{s_{E}} + \\frac{a^{2}}{8 \\chi_{L}}.\n$$\n\nPost-transition (high-confinement, with pedestal of width $\\Delta$) case:\n$$\n\\int_{0}^{a} \\frac{\\rho^{3}}{\\chi(\\rho)} \\, d\\rho = \\int_{0}^{a-\\Delta} \\frac{\\rho^{3}}{\\chi_{\\text{core}}} \\, d\\rho + \\int_{a-\\Delta}^{a} \\frac{\\rho^{3}}{\\chi_{\\text{ped}}} \\, d\\rho = \\frac{(a-\\Delta)^{4}}{4 \\chi_{\\text{core}}} + \\frac{a^{4} - (a-\\Delta)^{4}}{4 \\chi_{\\text{ped}}}.\n$$\nThus,\n$$\n\\tau_{E}^{(\\text{H})} = \\frac{\\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T_{\\text{edge}}}{s_{E}} + \\frac{1}{2 a^{2}} \\left[ \\frac{(a-\\Delta)^{4}}{4 \\chi_{\\text{core}}} + \\frac{a^{4} - (a-\\Delta)^{4}}{4 \\chi_{\\text{ped}}} \\right].\n$$\n\nThe improvement factor is\n$$\nF \\equiv \\frac{\\tau_{E}^{(\\text{H})}}{\\tau_{E}^{(\\text{L})}} = \\frac{\\frac{\\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T_{\\text{edge}}}{s_{E}} + \\frac{1}{2 a^{2}} \\left[ \\frac{(a-\\Delta)^{4}}{4 \\chi_{\\text{core}}} + \\frac{a^{4} - (a-\\Delta)^{4}}{4 \\chi_{\\text{ped}}} \\right]}{\\frac{\\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T_{\\text{edge}}}{s_{E}} + \\frac{a^{2}}{8 \\chi_{L}}}.\n$$\n\nNow insert the numerical values. First compute $V(a)$ and $s_{E}$:\n$$\nV(a) = 2 \\pi^{2} R a^{2} = 2 \\pi^{2} \\cdot 3.0 \\cdot 1.0^{2} = 6 \\pi^{2} \\,\\text{m}^{3},\n$$\n$$\ns_{E} = \\frac{P_{\\text{abs}}}{V(a)} = \\frac{12.0 \\times 10^{6} \\,\\text{W}}{6 \\pi^{2} \\,\\text{m}^{3}}.\n$$\nConvert $T_{\\text{edge}}$ to Kelvin:\n$$\nT_{\\text{edge}} = 150 \\times 11604.51812 \\,\\text{K} = 1.740677718 \\times 10^{6} \\,\\text{K}.\n$$\nDefine $c \\equiv \\frac{3}{2} k_{B}$ and compute the baseline term\n$$\n\\frac{\\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T_{\\text{edge}}}{s_{E}} = \\frac{c \\, n \\, T_{\\text{edge}}}{s_{E}}, \\quad \\text{with} \\quad c = \\frac{3}{2} k_{B} = 2.0709735 \\times 10^{-23} \\,\\text{J} \\,\\text{K}^{-1}.\n$$\nThus\n$$\nc n = 2.0709735 \\times 10^{-23} \\times 6.0 \\times 10^{19} = 1.2425841 \\times 10^{-3} \\,\\text{J} \\,\\text{K}^{-1} \\,\\text{m}^{-3},\n$$\n$$\nc n T_{\\text{edge}} = 1.2425841 \\times 10^{-3} \\times 1.740677718 \\times 10^{6} \\approx 2.16293847 \\times 10^{3} \\,\\text{J} \\,\\text{m}^{-3}.\n$$\nThe volume is $V(a) \\approx 6 \\pi^{2} \\approx 59.21762641 \\,\\text{m}^{3}$, so\n$$\ns_{E} = \\frac{12.0 \\times 10^{6}}{59.21762641} \\approx 2.026424 \\times 10^{5} \\,\\text{W} \\,\\text{m}^{-3}.\n$$\nTherefore,\n$$\n\\frac{\\frac{3}{2} n k_{B} T_{\\text{edge}}}{s_{E}} \\approx \\frac{2.16293847 \\times 10^{3}}{2.026424 \\times 10^{5}} \\approx 1.06768 \\times 10^{-2} \\,\\text{s}.\n$$\n\nCompute the transport integrals. For the low-confinement case,\n$$\n\\frac{a^{2}}{8 \\chi_{L}} = \\frac{1.0^{2}}{8 \\cdot 1.0} = 0.125 \\,\\text{s}.\n$$\nFor the high-confinement case with pedestal,\n$$\n(a - \\Delta) = 1.0 - 0.08 = 0.92,\n$$\n$$\n(a - \\Delta)^{4} = 0.92^{4} = (0.8464)^{2} = 0.71639296,\n$$\n$$\n\\frac{(a-\\Delta)^{4}}{4 \\chi_{\\text{core}}} = \\frac{0.71639296}{4 \\cdot 1.0} = 0.17909824,\n$$\n$$\n\\frac{a^{4} - (a-\\Delta)^{4}}{4 \\chi_{\\text{ped}}} = \\frac{1.0 - 0.71639296}{4 \\cdot 0.20} = \\frac{0.28360704}{0.80} = 0.35450880,\n$$\nso\n$$\n\\frac{1}{2 a^{2}} \\left[ \\frac{(a-\\Delta)^{4}}{4 \\chi_{\\text{core}}} + \\frac{a^{4} - (a-\\Delta)^{4}}{4 \\chi_{\\text{ped}}} \\right] = \\frac{1}{2} \\left( 0.17909824 + 0.35450880 \\right) = 0.26680352 \\,\\text{s}.\n$$\n\nAssemble the confinement times:\n$$\n\\tau_{E}^{(\\text{L})} \\approx 0.0106768 + 0.125 = 0.1356768 \\,\\text{s},\n$$\n$$\n\\tau_{E}^{(\\text{H})} \\approx 0.0106768 + 0.26680352 = 0.27748032 \\,\\text{s}.\n$$\nHence the improvement factor is\n$$\nF = \\frac{0.27748032}{0.1356768} \\approx 2.045.\n$$\nRounded to four significant figures, the dimensionless improvement factor is $2.045$."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{2.045}$$", "solution": "The problem is first validated and found to be scientifically grounded, well-posed, and objective, containing all necessary information for a unique solution. The model is a standard, albeit simplified, representation of energy transport in a tokamak plasma.\n\nThe derivation proceeds as follows. First, we establish the differential equation for the temperature profile $T(r)$. The total heating power enclosed within a flux surface of minor radius $r$ is $Q(r) = s_E V(r)$, where the uniform volumetric heating source is $s_E$ and the volume is $V(r) = 2 \\pi^2 R r^2$. The heat flux density across the surface area $S(r) = 4 \\pi^2 R r$ is thus:\n$$q(r) = \\frac{Q(r)}{S(r)} = \\frac{s_E (2 \\pi^2 R r^2)}{4 \\pi^2 R r} = \\frac{1}{2} s_E r$$\nThe total absorbed power is $P_{\\text{abs}} = s_E V(a) = s_E (2 \\pi^2 R a^2)$, which allows us to express $s_E$ as $s_E = \\frac{P_{\\text{abs}}}{2 \\pi^2 R a^2}$. Substituting this into the expression for $q(r)$:\n$$q(r) = \\frac{1}{2} \\left( \\frac{P_{\\text{abs}}}{2 \\pi^2 R a^2} \\right) r = \\frac{P_{\\text{abs}} r}{4 \\pi^2 R a^2}$$\nThis heat flux is driven by the temperature gradient according to the provided constitutive relation:\n$$q(r) = - \\frac{3}{2} n k_B \\chi(r) \\frac{dT}{dr}$$\nwhere $w(r) = \\frac{3}{2} n k_B T(r)$ is the thermal energy density, and $\\chi(r)$ is the energy diffusivity.\nEquating the two expressions for $q(r)$ yields the differential equation for the temperature profile $T(r)$:\n$$- \\frac{3}{2} n k_B \\chi(r) \\frac{dT}{dr} = \\frac{P_{\\text{abs}} r}{4 \\pi^2 R a^2}$$\n$$\\frac{dT}{dr} = - \\frac{P_{\\text{abs}} r}{6 \\pi^2 n k_B R a^2 \\chi(r)}$$\nInstead of solving for $T(r)$ directly, it is more elegant to work with the thermal energy density $w(r)$. The gradient of $w(r)$ is:\n$$\\frac{dw}{dr} = \\frac{3}{2} n k_B \\frac{dT}{dr} = - \\frac{q(r)}{\\chi(r)}$$\nIntegrating from a radius $r$ to the edge $a$:\n$$w(a) - w(r) = \\int_r^a \\frac{dw}{dr'} dr' = - \\int_r^a \\frac{q(r')}{\\chi(r')} dr'$$\nSo, the energy density profile is:\n$$w(r) = w(a) + \\int_r^a \\frac{q(r')}{\\chi(r')} dr' = w(a) + \\frac{P_{\\text{abs}}}{4 \\pi^2 R a^2} \\int_r^a \\frac{r'}{\\chi(r')} dr'$$\nwhere $w(a) = \\frac{3}{2} n k_B T_{\\text{edge}}$.\n\nThe total energy content $W$ is the volume integral of the energy density, $W = \\int w(r) dV$. The volume element is $dV = S(r) dr = 4 \\pi^2 R r dr$.\n$$W = \\int_0^a w(r) (4 \\pi^2 R r) dr = \\int_0^a \\left( w(a) + \\frac{P_{\\text{abs}}}{4 \\pi^2 R a^2} \\int_r^a \\frac{r'}{\\chi(r')} dr' \\right) (4 \\pi^2 R r) dr$$\n$$W = w(a) \\int_0^a 4 \\pi^2 R r dr + \\frac{P_{\\text{abs}}}{a^2} \\int_0^a r \\left( \\int_r^a \\frac{r'}{\\chi(r')} dr' \\right) dr$$\nThe first term is $w(a) (2 \\pi^2 R a^2) = w(a) V(a)$. The second term can be simplified using integration by parts, $\\int u \\, dv = uv - \\int v \\, du$, with $u = \\int_r^a \\frac{r'}{\\chi(r')} dr'$ and $dv = r dr$. This gives $du = -\\frac{r}{\\chi(r)} dr$ and $v = \\frac{r^2}{2}$.\n$$\\int_0^a r \\left( \\int_r^a \\frac{r'}{\\chi(r')} dr' \\right) dr = \\left[ \\frac{r^2}{2} \\int_r^a \\frac{r'}{\\chi(r')} dr' \\right]_0^a - \\int_0^a \\frac{r^2}{2} \\left(-\\frac{r}{\\chi(r)}\\right) dr = 0 + \\frac{1}{2} \\int_0^a \\frac{r^3}{\\chi(r)} dr$$\nThe boundary term vanishes at both limits. Thus, the total energy is:\n$$W = w(a) V(a) + \\frac{P_{\\text{abs}}}{2a^2} \\int_0^a \\frac{r^3}{\\chi(r)} dr$$\nThe global energy confinement time is $\\tau_E = W / P_{\\text{abs}}$:\n$$\\tau_E = \\frac{w(a)V(a)}{P_{\\text{abs}}} + \\frac{1}{2a^2} \\int_0^a \\frac{r^3}{\\chi(r)} dr$$\nSubstituting $w(a) = \\frac{3}{2} n k_B T_{\\text{edge}}$ and $V(a) = 2 \\pi^2 R a^2$, we get the general expression for $\\tau_E$:\n$$\\tau_E = \\frac{3 \\pi^2 n k_B R a^2 T_{\\text{edge}}}{P_{\\text{abs}}} + \\frac{1}{2a^2} \\int_0^a \\frac{r^3}{\\chi(r)} dr$$\n\nNow, we apply this formula to the L-mode and H-mode cases. Let the first term, which is common to both, be $\\tau_{\\text{edge}}$.\n\nFor the L-mode (pre-transition), $\\chi(r) = \\chi_L$ is constant for $0 \\le r \\le a$.\n$$\\int_0^a \\frac{r^3}{\\chi_L} dr = \\frac{1}{\\chi_L} \\left[ \\frac{r^4}{4} \\right]_0^a = \\frac{a^4}{4\\chi_L}$$\n$$\\tau_E^{(L)} = \\tau_{\\text{edge}} + \\frac{1}{2a^2} \\left( \\frac{a^4}{4\\chi_L} \\right) = \\tau_{\\text{edge}} + \\frac{a^2}{8\\chi_L}$$\n\nFor the H-mode (post-transition), $\\chi(r)$ is piecewise: $\\chi_{\\text{core}}$ for $0 \\le r \\le a - \\Delta$ and $\\chi_{\\text{ped}}$ for $a - \\Delta < r \\le a$. The integral becomes:\n$$\\int_0^a \\frac{r^3}{\\chi(r)} dr = \\int_0^{a-\\Delta} \\frac{r^3}{\\chi_{\\text{core}}} dr + \\int_{a-\\Delta}^a \\frac{r^3}{\\chi_{\\text{ped}}} dr$$\n$$= \\frac{1}{\\chi_{\\text{core}}} \\left[ \\frac{r^4}{4} \\right]_0^{a-\\Delta} + \\frac{1}{\\chi_{\\text{ped}}} \\left[ \\frac{r^4}{4} \\right]_{a-\\Delta}^a = \\frac{(a-\\Delta)^4}{4\\chi_{\\text{core}}} + \\frac{a^4 - (a-\\Delta)^4}{4\\chi_{\\text{ped}}}$$\n$$\\tau_E^{(H)} = \\tau_{\\text{edge}} + \\frac{1}{2a^2} \\left( \\frac{(a-\\Delta)^4}{4\\chi_{\\text{core}}} + \\frac{a^4 - (a-\\Delta)^4}{4\\chi_{\\text{ped}}} \\right) = \\tau_{\\text{edge}} + \\frac{1}{8a^2} \\left( \\frac{(a-\\Delta)^4}{\\chi_{\\text{core}}} + \\frac{a^4 - (a-\\Delta)^4}{\\chi_{\\text{ped}}} \\right)$$\n\nGiven that $\\chi_L = \\chi_{\\text{core}}$, the improvement factor $F = \\tau_E^{(H)}/\\tau_E^{(L)}$ is:\n$$F = \\frac{\\tau_{\\text{edge}} + \\frac{1}{8a^2} \\left( \\frac{(a-\\Delta)^4}{\\chi_{\\text{core}}} + \\frac{a^4 - (a-\\Delta)^4}{\\chi_{\\text{ped}}} \\right)}{\\tau_{\\text{edge}} + \\frac{a^2}{8\\chi_{\\text{core}}}}$$\nWe now substitute the numerical values provided.\nFirst, we calculate $\\tau_{\\text{edge}}$:\n$T_{\\text{edge}} = 150 \\,\\text{eV} \\times 11604.51812 \\,\\text{K/eV} = 1740677.718 \\,\\text{K}$.\nWe use $k_B = 1.380649 \\times 10^{-23} \\,\\text{J/K}$.\n$k_B T_{\\text{edge}} = (1.380649 \\times 10^{-23} \\,\\text{J/K}) \\times (1740677.718 \\,\\text{K}) \\approx 2.403265 \\times 10^{-17} \\,\\text{J}$.\n$$\\tau_{\\text{edge}} = \\frac{3 \\pi^2 (6.0 \\times 10^{19} \\,\\text{m}^{-3}) (1.380649 \\times 10^{-23} \\,\\text{J/K}) (3.0 \\,\\text{m}) (1.0 \\,\\text{m})^2 (150 \\times 11604.51812 \\,\\text{K})}{12.0 \\times 10^6 \\,\\text{W}}$$\n$$\\tau_{\\text{edge}} = \\frac{3 \\pi^2 (6.0 \\times 10^{19}) (2.403265 \\times 10^{-17}) (3.0) (1.0)^2}{12.0 \\times 10^6} \\approx \\frac{128243.6}{12.0 \\times 10^6} \\approx 0.010687 \\,\\text{s}$$\nNext, we calculate the incremental confinement times.\nFor L-mode, with $a = 1.0 \\,\\text{m}$ and $\\chi_L = \\chi_{\\text{core}} = 1.0 \\,\\text{m}^2/\\text{s}$:\n$$\\tau_{\\text{inc}}^{(L)} = \\frac{a^2}{8\\chi_{\\text{core}}} = \\frac{(1.0)^2}{8(1.0)} = 0.125 \\,\\text{s}$$\nSo, $\\tau_E^{(L)} = \\tau_{\\text{edge}} + \\tau_{\\text{inc}}^{(L)} = 0.010687 + 0.125 = 0.135687 \\,\\text{s}$.\n\nFor H-mode, with $a = 1.0 \\,\\text{m}$, $\\Delta = 0.08 \\,\\text{m}$, $\\chi_{\\text{core}} = 1.0 \\,\\text{m}^2/\\text{s}$, and $\\chi_{\\text{ped}} = 0.20 \\,\\text{m}^2/\\text{s}$:\n$$\\tau_{\\text{inc}}^{(H)} = \\frac{1}{8(1.0)^2} \\left( \\frac{(1.0 - 0.08)^4}{1.0} + \\frac{(1.0)^4 - (1.0 - 0.08)^4}{0.20} \\right)$$\n$$\\tau_{\\text{inc}}^{(H)} = \\frac{1}{8} \\left( (0.92)^4 + \\frac{1 - (0.92)^4}{0.20} \\right)$$\nUsing $(0.92)^4 \\approx 0.716393$:\n$$\\tau_{\\text{inc}}^{(H)} \\approx \\frac{1}{8} \\left( 0.716393 + \\frac{1 - 0.716393}{0.20} \\right) = \\frac{1}{8} \\left( 0.716393 + \\frac{0.283607}{0.20} \\right)$$\n$$\\tau_{\\text{inc}}^{(H)} \\approx \\frac{1}{8} (0.716393 + 1.418035) = \\frac{2.134428}{8} \\approx 0.266804 \\,\\text{s}$$\nSo, $\\tau_E^{(H)} = \\tau_{\\text{edge}} + \\tau_{\\text{inc}}^{(H)} = 0.010687 + 0.266804 = 0.277491 \\,\\text{s}$.\n\nFinally, we compute the improvement factor $F$:\n$$F = \\frac{\\tau_E^{(H)}}{\\tau_E^{(L)}} = \\frac{0.277491}{0.135687} \\approx 2.04508$$\nRounding to four significant figures, we get $F = 2.045$."}]


title: ...deriving the modified threshold power for catastrophic self-focusing in a medium that exhibits both a Kerr effect and two-photon absorption.

Question: In nonlinear optics, an intense laser beam propagating through a Kerr medium can experience self-focusing. The refractive index of the medium is given by $n = n_0 + n_2 I$, where $n_0$ is the linear refractive index, $I$ is the beam intensity, and $n_2$ is the nonlinear refractive index coefficient. For an input power $P$ exceeding a critical power $P_{cr0}$, the self-focusing effect overcomes natural diffraction, leading to a catastrophic collapse of the beam.

This phenomenon is complicated by other nonlinear effects, such as two-photon absorption (TPA), where the absorption coefficient is intensity-dependent: $\alpha_{TPA} = \beta I$. TPA leads to power loss, which can counteract self-focusing and arrest the beam collapse.

A simplified model for the threshold power $P_{th}$ in a medium with both Kerr effect and TPA can be established by considering the interplay between the self-focusing distance and the TPA-induced power loss. The self-focusing distance $z_f$ for a beam with input power $P_{in}$ is given by the formula:
$$ z_f = \frac{L_D}{\sqrt{\frac{P_{in}}{P_{cr0}} - 1}} $$
where $L_D = \frac{\pi n_0 w_0^2}{\lambda_0}$ is the diffraction length for a Gaussian beam with initial radius $w_0$ and vacuum wavelength $\lambda_0$.

The power loss due to TPA can be characterized by an effective absorption length $L_{TPA}$, over which the beam power is significantly depleted. For a beam with power $P_{in}$, this length is modeled as:
$$ L_{TPA} = \frac{\pi w_0^2}{\beta P_{in}} $$
The threshold for catastrophic self-focusing is modified by TPA. The new threshold power $P_{th}$ is defined as the input power for which the beam power attenuates to exactly $P_{cr0}$ over the self-focusing distance $z_f$. The power attenuation along the propagation axis $z$ is modeled by $P(z) = P_{th} \exp(-z/L_{TPA})$.

This definition leads to a transcendental equation for the normalized threshold power, $p_{th} = P_{th}/P_{cr0}$:
$$ \frac{\ln(p_{th})}{p_{th}} = \frac{\kappa}{\sqrt{p_{th}-1}} $$
where $\kappa = \frac{n_0 \beta P_{cr0}}{\lambda_0}$ is a dimensionless parameter that quantifies the strength of TPA relative to the Kerr effect.

Assuming that the TPA is a weak effect ($\kappa \ll 1$), the modified threshold power $P_{th}$ will be only slightly larger than $P_{cr0}$. Derive an approximate closed-form expression for the modified threshold power $P_{th}$ in terms of $P_{cr0}$ and $\kappa$, valid to the lowest non-vanishing order in $\kappa$.



Answer: [{"answer": "$$ \\boxed{P_{cr0} \\left(1 + \\kappa^{2/3}\\right)} $$", "solution": "The problem asks for an approximate expression for the modified threshold power $P_{th}$ in the presence of weak two-photon absorption (TPA). The starting point is the transcendental equation relating the normalized threshold power $p_{th} = P_{th}/P_{cr0}$ to the dimensionless TPA parameter $\\kappa$:\n$$ \\frac{\\ln(p_{th})}{p_{th}} = \\frac{\\kappa}{\\sqrt{p_{th}-1}} $$\nWe are given that TPA is a weak effect, which implies $\\kappa \\ll 1$. In this limit, the modified threshold power $P_{th}$ is expected to be close to the TPA-free critical power $P_{cr0}$. This means that the normalized threshold power $p_{th}$ is close to 1.\n\nLet's define a small dimensionless quantity $\\epsilon$ such that:\n$$ p_{th} = 1 + \\epsilon $$\nSince $p_{th}$ is slightly greater than 1, $\\epsilon$ is a small positive number ($\\epsilon > 0$). The condition $\\kappa \\ll 1$ suggests that $\\epsilon \\ll 1$. Our goal is to find an expression for $\\epsilon$ in terms of $\\kappa$, and then substitute it back to find $P_{th}$.\n\nWe substitute $p_{th} = 1 + \\epsilon$ into the transcendental equation.\nFirst, let's analyze the left-hand side (LHS) of the equation:\n$$ \\text{LHS} = \\frac{\\ln(1+\\epsilon)}{1+\\epsilon} $$\nUsing the Taylor series expansion for $\\ln(1+\\epsilon)$ for small $\\epsilon$:\n$$ \\ln(1+\\epsilon) = \\epsilon - \\frac{\\epsilon^2}{2} + \\frac{\\epsilon^3}{3} - \\dots $$\nAnd for the denominator, we use the geometric series expansion $(1+\\epsilon)^{-1}$:\n$$ \\frac{1}{1+\\epsilon} = 1 - \\epsilon + \\epsilon^2 - \\dots $$\nMultiplying these two series, we get:\n$$ \\text{LHS} = \\left(\\epsilon - \\frac{\\epsilon^2}{2} + O(\\epsilon^3)\\right) (1 - \\epsilon + O(\\epsilon^2)) $$\n$$ \\text{LHS} = \\epsilon - \\epsilon^2 - \\frac{\\epsilon^2}{2} + O(\\epsilon^3) = \\epsilon - \\frac{3}{2}\\epsilon^2 + O(\\epsilon^3) $$\nTo the lowest order in $\\epsilon$, the LHS is simply $\\epsilon$.\n\nNow, let's analyze the right-hand side (RHS) of the equation:\n$$ \\text{RHS} = \\frac{\\kappa}{\\sqrt{(1+\\epsilon)-1}} = \\frac{\\kappa}{\\sqrt{\\epsilon}} = \\kappa \\epsilon^{-1/2} $$\nEquating the lowest-order terms of the LHS and RHS:\n$$ \\epsilon \\approx \\kappa \\epsilon^{-1/2} $$\nThis equation relates the small parameters $\\epsilon$ and $\\kappa$. We can solve for $\\epsilon$:\n$$ \\epsilon^{1 + 1/2} \\approx \\kappa $$\n$$ \\epsilon^{3/2} \\approx \\kappa $$\n$$ \\epsilon \\approx \\kappa^{2/3} $$\nThis result is consistent with our initial assumption that $\\epsilon \\ll 1$ when $\\kappa \\ll 1$.\n\nNow, we substitute this expression for $\\epsilon$ back into the definition of $p_{th}$:\n$$ p_{th} = 1 + \\epsilon \\approx 1 + \\kappa^{2/3} $$\nFinally, we express the modified threshold power $P_{th}$ in terms of $P_{cr0}$ and $\\kappa$:\n$$ P_{th} = p_{th} P_{cr0} = P_{cr0} (1 + \\kappa^{2/3}) $$\nThis is the approximate closed-form expression for the modified threshold power, valid to the lowest non-vanishing order in the TPA parameter $\\kappa$."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{P_{th}=P_{cr0}\\bigl(1+\\kappa^{2/3}\\bigr)}$$", "solution": "We start from the transcendental condition for the normalized threshold power $p_{th}=P_{th}/P_{cr0}$:\n$$\\frac{\\ln p_{th}}{p_{th}}=\\frac{\\kappa}{\\sqrt{p_{th}-1}}\\,. \\tag1$$\nSet $p_{th}=1+\\epsilon$ with $\\epsilon\\ll1$. Then\n$$\\ln(1+\\epsilon)=\\epsilon-\\tfrac12\\epsilon^2+O(\\epsilon^3),\\quad\n\\frac1{1+\\epsilon}=1-\\epsilon+O(\\epsilon^2),$$\nso\n$$\\frac{\\ln(1+\\epsilon)}{1+\\epsilon}\n=\\bigl(\\epsilon-\\tfrac12\\epsilon^2+O(\\epsilon^3)\\bigr)\\bigl(1-\\epsilon+O(\\epsilon^2)\\bigr)\n=\\epsilon-\\tfrac32\\epsilon^2+O(\\epsilon^3)\\,. \\tag2$$\nMeanwhile\n$$\\sqrt{p_{th}-1}=\\sqrt{\\epsilon},\\quad\n\\frac{\\kappa}{\\sqrt{p_{th}-1}}=\\frac{\\kappa}{\\sqrt{\\epsilon}}\\,. \\tag3$$\nEquating (2) and (3) gives\n$$\\epsilon-\\tfrac32\\epsilon^2=\\frac{\\kappa}{\\sqrt{\\epsilon}}+O(\\epsilon^{3/2})\\,. \\tag4$$\nAssume an expansion\n$$\\epsilon=A\\,\\kappa^{2/3}+B\\,\\kappa^{4/3}+O(\\kappa^2)\\,. \\tag5$$\nThen\n$$\\epsilon-\\tfrac32\\epsilon^2\n=A\\kappa^{2/3}+\\bigl(B-\\tfrac32A^2\\bigr)\\kappa^{4/3}+O(\\kappa^2),$$\n$$\\frac{\\kappa}{\\sqrt{\\epsilon}}\n=\\frac{\\kappa}{\\sqrt{A}\\,\\kappa^{1/3}}\\Bigl(1-\\tfrac{B}{2A}\\kappa^{2/3}+O(\\kappa^{4/3})\\Bigr)\n=\\frac1{\\sqrt{A}}\\kappa^{2/3}-\\frac{B}{2A\\sqrt{A}}\\kappa^{4/3}+O(\\kappa^2)\\,. $$\nMatching $\\kappa^{2/3}$ and $\\kappa^{4/3}$ terms yields $A=1$ and $B=1$. Hence\n$$\\epsilon=\\kappa^{2/3}+O(\\kappa^{4/3}),\\quad\np_{th}=1+\\kappa^{2/3}+O(\\kappa^{4/3}).$$\nTo lowest non‐vanishing order,\n$$P_{th}=P_{cr0}\\,p_{th}\\approx P_{cr0}\\bigl(1+\\kappa^{2/3}\\bigr)\\,. $$"}]


title: Given a particle's velocity vector $\vec{v}$ on a rotating platform with angular velocity $\vec{\omega}$, find the direction of the Coriolis acceleration vector.

Question: A large, perfectly flat, circular platform serves as a stage for a physics demonstration. The platform rotates with a constant angular velocity about a fixed vertical axis passing through its center. A Cartesian coordinate system $(x,y,z)$ is defined such that it is fixed to the rotating platform. Its origin $(0,0,0)$ is at the center of the platform, the $xy$-plane coincides with the platform's surface, and the positive $z$-axis points vertically upwards, aligned with the axis of rotation.

The platform rotates at a constant angular speed of $\omega_0 = 2.5 \text{ rad/s}$ in a counter-clockwise direction when viewed from a point high above the positive $z$-axis. At a specific moment, a small, frictionless puck is observed to be sliding across the platform. An observer also on the platform measures the puck's velocity relative to the platform to be $\vec{v} = (3.0 \hat{i} + 4.0 \hat{j}) \text{ m/s}$.

Calculate the Coriolis acceleration vector, $\vec{a}_c$, experienced by the puck at this instant. Express your answer as the ordered components $(a_x, a_y, a_z)$ in units of m/s².



Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 20.0 & -15.0 & 0 \\end{pmatrix}}$$", "solution": "The Coriolis acceleration, $\\vec{a}_c$, is an apparent acceleration that acts on objects in motion within a rotating frame of reference. It is given by the formula:\n$$\n\\vec{a}_c = -2(\\vec{\\omega} \\times \\vec{v})\n$$\nwhere $\\vec{\\omega}$ is the angular velocity vector of the rotating frame, and $\\vec{v}$ is the velocity vector of the object as measured within that rotating frame.\n\nFirst, we must determine the angular velocity vector, $\\vec{\\omega}$, from the problem description. The platform rotates about the vertical $z$-axis. The right-hand rule for rotation states that if you curl the fingers of your right hand in the direction of rotation, your thumb points in the direction of the angular velocity vector. Since the platform rotates counter-clockwise in the $xy$-plane (when viewed from above), the angular velocity vector points in the positive $z$-direction. The magnitude is given as $\\omega_0 = 2.5 \\text{ rad/s}$. Therefore, the angular velocity vector is:\n$$\n\\vec{\\omega} = 0 \\hat{i} + 0 \\hat{j} + 2.5 \\hat{k} \\text{ rad/s}\n$$\n\nNext, we identify the velocity of the puck relative to the rotating frame, which is given in the problem statement:\n$$\n\\vec{v} = 3.0 \\hat{i} + 4.0 \\hat{j} + 0 \\hat{k} \\text{ m/s}\n$$\n\nNow, we compute the cross product $\\vec{\\omega} \\times \\vec{v}$. We can use the determinant method for calculating the cross product of two vectors in Cartesian coordinates:\n$$\n\\vec{\\omega} \\times \\vec{v} = \\begin{vmatrix}\n\\hat{i} & \\hat{j} & \\hat{k} \\\\\n\\omega_x & \\omega_y & \\omega_z \\\\\nv_x & v_y & v_z\n\\end{vmatrix}\n$$\nSubstituting the components of our vectors:\n$$\n\\vec{\\omega} \\times \\vec{v} = \\begin{vmatrix}\n\\hat{i} & \\hat{j} & \\hat{k} \\\\\n0 & 0 & 2.5 \\\\\n3.0 & 4.0 & 0\n\\end{vmatrix}\n$$\nWe evaluate the determinant:\n$$\n\\vec{\\omega} \\times \\vec{v} = \\hat{i}( (0)(0) - (2.5)(4.0) ) - \\hat{j}( (0)(0) - (2.5)(3.0) ) + \\hat{k}( (0)(4.0) - (0)(3.0) )\n$$\n$$\n\\vec{\\omega} \\times \\vec{v} = \\hat{i}(0 - 10.0) - \\hat{j}(0 - 7.5) + \\hat{k}(0 - 0)\n$$\n$$\n\\vec{\\omega} \\times \\vec{v} = -10.0 \\hat{i} + 7.5 \\hat{j} + 0 \\hat{k}\n$$\nThe units of this cross product are $(\\text{rad/s}) \\times (\\text{m/s}) = \\text{m/s}^2$, since radians are dimensionless.\n\nFinally, we substitute this result back into the formula for the Coriolis acceleration:\n$$\n\\vec{a}_c = -2(\\vec{\\omega} \\times \\vec{v})\n$$\n$$\n\\vec{a}_c = -2(-10.0 \\hat{i} + 7.5 \\hat{j})\n$$\n$$\n\\vec{a}_c = 20.0 \\hat{i} - 15.0 \\hat{j} + 0 \\hat{k} \\text{ m/s}^2\n$$\nThe components of the Coriolis acceleration vector are therefore $(a_x, a_y, a_z) = (20.0, -15.0, 0)$."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix}20 & -15 & 0\\end{pmatrix}}$$", "solution": "We work in the frame rigidly attached to the rotating platform. In a uniformly rotating frame with angular velocity vector $\\vec{\\Omega}$, the Coriolis acceleration of a particle with velocity relative to the rotating frame $\\vec{v}$ is given by\n$$\n\\vec{a}_{c}=-2\\,\\vec{\\Omega}\\times\\vec{v}.\n$$\nThe platform rotates counter-clockwise about the $z$-axis when viewed from $+z$, so the angular velocity vector is\n$$\n\\vec{\\Omega}=\\omega_{0}\\,\\hat{k},\\quad \\omega_{0}=2.5.\n$$\nThe puck’s velocity relative to the platform is\n$$\n\\vec{v}=3.0\\,\\hat{i}+4.0\\,\\hat{j}.\n$$\nCompute the cross product:\n$$\n\\vec{\\Omega}\\times\\vec{v}=\n\\begin{vmatrix}\n\\hat{i} & \\hat{j} & \\hat{k} \\\\\n0 & 0 & \\omega_{0} \\\\\n3.0 & 4.0 & 0\n\\end{vmatrix}\n=\\hat{i}\\,(0\\cdot 0-\\omega_{0}\\cdot 4.0)-\\hat{j}\\,(0\\cdot 0-\\omega_{0}\\cdot 3.0)+\\hat{k}\\,(0\\cdot 4.0-0\\cdot 3.0)\n=(-4.0\\,\\omega_{0})\\,\\hat{i}+(3.0\\,\\omega_{0})\\,\\hat{j}+0\\,\\hat{k}.\n$$\nTherefore,\n$$\n\\vec{a}_{c}=-2\\,\\vec{\\Omega}\\times\\vec{v}=-2\\,\\bigl[(-4.0\\,\\omega_{0})\\,\\hat{i}+(3.0\\,\\omega_{0})\\,\\hat{j}\\bigr]\n=(8.0\\,\\omega_{0})\\,\\hat{i}+(-6.0\\,\\omega_{0})\\,\\hat{j}.\n$$\nSubstitute $\\omega_{0}=2.5$ to obtain\n$$\n\\vec{a}_{c}=(20,\\,-15,\\,0).\n$$"}]


title: [multiple_choice] Define edge localized modes and distinguish Type I, II, and III ELMs in terms of collisionality, pedestal pressure, and frequency, stating their qualitative triggers.

Question: A toroidal plasma in a High-confinement mode (H-mode) forms a pedestal where the edge pressure gradient is steep and transport is reduced. In a simple magnetohydrodynamic (MHD) description, local force balance near the edge obeys $\nabla p = \mathbf{J} \times \mathbf{B}$, and stability against interchange and ballooning-like perturbations is governed by the pressure gradient and magnetic geometry, often summarized by parameters such as the safety factor $q$ and the local magnetic shear. The pedestal supports a bootstrap current $j_{\mathrm{bs}}$ that originates from collisional friction between trapped and passing particles; at fixed gradient, $j_{\mathrm{bs}}$ strengthens as electron collisionality decreases, which can be expressed by the normalized electron collisionality $\nu^{\ast}$ defined as a dimensionless ratio of the characteristic electron-electron collision frequency to a characteristic transit frequency. Ideal peeling-ballooning modes are driven by the combination of edge current density $j_{\parallel}$ and the pressure gradient $\partial p / \partial r$, whereas resistive ballooning and drift-wave-like instabilities are facilitated when resistivity (and thus collisionality) is high. The pedestal relaxes quasi-periodically through Edge Localized Modes (ELMs), which expel particles and energy, resetting $p_{\mathrm{ped}}$ and thus regulating the edge transport.

Using only the foregoing base—MHD force balance, the role of $\nu^{\ast}$ in $j_{\mathrm{bs}}$, and the qualitative distinction between ideal peeling-ballooning and resistive or drift-wave edge instabilities—select the option that most accurately defines Edge Localized Modes (ELMs) and distinguishes Type I, Type II, and Type III ELMs in terms of normalized collisionality $\nu^{\ast}$, pedestal pressure $p_{\mathrm{ped}}$, burst frequency $f_{\mathrm{ELM}}$, and qualitative trigger, including the geometric conditions that are known to favor Type II behavior.

A. Edge Localized Modes (ELMs) are quasi-periodic edge instabilities that relax the H-mode pedestal by expelling particles and heat, thereby regulating $p_{\mathrm{ped}}$. Type I ELMs occur at low $\nu^{\ast}$ with high $p_{\mathrm{ped}}$ and relatively low $f_{\mathrm{ELM}}$, and are triggered when the edge bootstrap current and pressure gradient cross the ideal peeling-ballooning stability boundary. Type II ELMs are very high-frequency, small-amplitude events that appear in strongly shaped, near double-null plasmas (e.g., high triangularity) at low-to-intermediate $\nu^{\ast}$, maintaining moderate-to-high $p_{\mathrm{ped}}$ by continuous benign edge activity typically attributed to high-$n$ ballooning-like or kinetic edge modes. Type III ELMs are high-frequency, small bursts at high $\nu^{\ast}$ with low $p_{\mathrm{ped}}$, commonly associated with resistive ballooning or drift-wave turbulence when input power is insufficient to sustain the high pedestal, near the transition back toward Low-confinement mode.

B. Edge Localized Modes (ELMs) are core MHD disruptions triggered by central current profile oscillations. Type I ELMs occur at high $\nu^{\ast}$ with low $p_{\mathrm{ped}}$ and high $f_{\mathrm{ELM}}$, driven primarily by resistive ballooning modes. Type II ELMs are low-frequency, large-amplitude bursts that arise in double-null plasmas at high $\nu^{\ast}$ with low $p_{\mathrm{ped}}$, triggered by peeling instabilities. Type III ELMs happen at low $\nu^{\ast}$ with high $p_{\mathrm{ped}}$, and are ideal peeling-ballooning events.

C. Edge Localized Modes (ELMs) are transient pedestal relaxations that expel edge plasma. Type I ELMs are driven at high $\nu^{\ast}$ with high $f_{\mathrm{ELM}}$ and low $p_{\mathrm{ped}}$ by resistive drift-wave instabilities. Type II ELMs occur at moderate $\nu^{\ast}$ and low $f_{\mathrm{ELM}}$, require low triangularity single-null plasmas, and are triggered by peeling-ballooning instabilities. Type III ELMs arise at low $\nu^{\ast}$ with high $p_{\mathrm{ped}}$ and low $f_{\mathrm{ELM}}$, associated with ideal ballooning.

D. Edge Localized Modes (ELMs) are pedestal events that reduce core pressure. Type I ELMs occur at low $\nu^{\ast}$ with high $p_{\mathrm{ped}}$ and low $f_{\mathrm{ELM}}$, but are triggered by resistive ballooning and drift-wave turbulence. Type II ELMs require low shaping and low edge collisionality, produce low-frequency large bursts, and are peeling instabilities. Type III ELMs are high-frequency small bursts at low $\nu^{\ast}$ and high $p_{\mathrm{ped}}$, generated by ideal peeling-ballooning activity.

Choose the single best option.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "The pedestal arises in High-confinement mode through reduced edge transport. The force balance $\\nabla p = \\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B}$ indicates that changes in $\\nabla p$ must be balanced by $\\mathbf{J}$ and $\\mathbf{B}$, and stability is a function of both pressure gradient and current profile. The bootstrap current density $j_{\\mathrm{bs}}$ scales with the edge pressure gradient and depends inversely on collisional friction; as electron collisionality $\\nu^{\\ast}$ decreases, trapped particle effects strengthen and $j_{\\mathrm{bs}}$ increases. Ideal peeling-ballooning instabilities therefore tend to be destabilized when both the edge current $j_{\\parallel}$ (including $j_{\\mathrm{bs}}$) and the pressure gradient $\\partial p / \\partial r$ are large, a condition more easily met at low $\\nu^{\\ast}$ and high pedestal pressure $p_{\\mathrm{ped}}$. The resulting ELMs are relatively infrequent but large in amplitude, since the pedestal accumulates energy between relaxations.\n\nWhen collisionality $\\nu^{\\ast}$ is high, resistivity increases and ideal MHD criteria are relaxed, while resistive ballooning and drift-wave-like modes are more easily excited. At high $\\nu^{\\ast}$ and low input power, the pedestal cannot reach high $p_{\\mathrm{ped}}$, and the system sheds energy through more frequent, smaller events, often called Type III ELMs. These are associated with resistive edge instabilities and high-frequency, small-amplitude bursts.\n\nType II ELMs, often dubbed “grassy” ELMs, are observed in highly shaped plasmas, commonly near double-null configurations and high triangularity. The edge geometry modifies the stability boundary, and benign high-$n$ edge modes (including kinetic ballooning-like activity) can continuously regulate the pedestal. The resulting ELM signature is very high-frequency small bursts, with $p_{\\mathrm{ped}}$ maintained at moderate-to-high levels. The collisionality is typically low-to-intermediate; these are not resistive-dominated events, and they are distinct from Type III behavior.\n\nWith this reasoning, we evaluate each option.\n\nOption A:\n- Definition: ELMs are quasi-periodic edge instabilities that relax the pedestal by expelling particles and heat. This matches standard understanding.\n- Type I: Low $\\nu^{\\ast}$, high $p_{\\mathrm{ped}}$, low $f_{\\mathrm{ELM}}$, trigger by ideal peeling-ballooning boundary crossing. This aligns with the peeling-ballooning paradigm.\n- Type II: Very high-frequency, small-amplitude, near double-null/high triangularity, low-to-intermediate $\\nu^{\\ast}$, maintains moderate-to-high $p_{\\mathrm{ped}}$, with benign high-$n$ or kinetic edge modes regulating transport. This captures the widely reported phenomenology of “grassy” ELMs without asserting a resistive trigger.\n- Type III: High $\\nu^{\\ast}$, low $p_{\\mathrm{ped}}$, high $f_{\\mathrm{ELM}}$, resistive ballooning/drift-wave turbulence, often near the back transition when input power is insufficient. This matches the conventional description.\nVerdict — Correct.\n\nOption B:\n- Claims ELMs are core MHD disruptions driven by central current oscillations. This incorrectly swaps Type III with Type I.\nVerdict — Incorrect.\n\nOption C:\n- Defines ELMs generically as transient pedestal relaxations; acceptable in spirit, but the classifications that follow are flawed.\n- Maps Type I to high $\\nu^{\\ast}$, high $f_{\\mathrm{ELM}}$, low $p_{\\mathrm{ped}}$ by resistive drift-wave modes, contradicting the ideal peeling-ballooning nature at low triangularity/double-null with very high frequency small bursts.\n- Maps Type III to low $\\nu^{\\ast}$, high $p_{\\mathrm{ped}}$, low $f_{\\mathrm{ELM}}$, again swapping with Type I characteristics.\nVerdict — Incorrect.\n\nOption D:\n- States Type I occur at low $\\nu^{\\ast}$ with high $p_{\\mathrm{ped}}$ and low $f_{\\mathrm{ELM}}$, but claims a resistive ballooning/drift-wave
